YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
SUMhARY
i.        This report appraises a project in the Socialist Federal Republic
of Yugoslavia for agricultural development in the Republic of Macedonia. The
proposed IBRD loan of US$31 million (50% of total project cost) would cover
the foreign exchange component of about US$18 million equivlent (29% of total
project cost), as well as US$13 million of local currency requirements (21%
of total project cost), and would serve to mobilize an additional US$31 mil-
lion within the country to meet the total cost of the project--approximately
US$62 million. The proposed loan would be made to the Stopanska Bank (SBS),
Macedonia, w th the guarantee of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia.
ii.       The Republic of Macedonia constitutes about 10% of Yugoslavia's
total land area and has a population of 1.7 million which is about 8% of the
country's total population. Macedonia's agricultural sector employs about
47% of its population and contributes about 28% to its GNP. The social sec-
tor consists of socially-owned, self managed enterprises (combinats) which
produce mainly wheat, sugar, grapes, wine and livestock products. This pro-
duction is usually vertically integrated with the combinats own processing
and marketing facilities. The combinats account for only 20% of Macedonia's
total arable land but contribute about 30% of all agricultural income. The
private-sector accounts for the remaining 70% of agricultural income and
includes 160,000 private farmers, of whom 7,500 are organized into about 170
farmers' cooperatives.
{ii.      The average private holding is only 3 ha and the level of tech-
nology is low compared with the social sector. Private farmers procure
their inputs and market their produce either through their cooperatives or
through combinats with which they often have contracts to produce vegetables,
fruits, and other products which tend to be labor-intensive. A government
extension service has been established recently in Macedonia to provide
technical assistance to private farmers. In the past, such technical advice
was available only through combinats and cooperatives, an arrangement which
has been only partly effective.
iv.       The project would include both the social and private farm sectors
in the Republic of Macedonia. In the social sector, it would help finance
the development or construction of:
(a) livestock production--dairy cattle, beef cattle for fatten-
ing and rearing pigs;
(b) a modern meat processing plant with an annual production of
about 11,000 tons of fresh and processed meats;
(c) 2,600 ha of vineyards;
0



- ii -
(d) wine processing facilities--42 million liters production of
wine and carbonated drinks annually;
(e)  wine by-product processing facilities;
(f)  cold storage and grain silo facilities; and
(g)  fish ponds to produce about 1,500 tons of carp and 75 tons
of trout annually.
In the private sector the project would help finance:
(a) construction of about 150 tobacco drying sheds; and
(b) on-farm improvements--mainly for tobacco, table grapes and
dairy farms.
In addition, the project includes technical studies for private farm pro-
ductivity improvement programs.
v.        The procurement of the meat, wine and wine by-products processing
facilities would be subject to international competitive bidding for a total
value of about US$13.5 million equivalent. Heifer purchases, equivalent to
about US$3.1 million, would be obtained after receiving bid quotations from
at least three countries.  Local bidding would apply for other project in-
vestment items where the value of such items for any subcontract exceeded
US$20,000 equivalent. All remaining investment items under the project
would be procured through normal commercial channels.
vi.       The Agriculture and Forestry Loans Department of SBS would be re-
sponsible for carrying out the project. As IBRD has apprais~ed all subproj-
ects, SBS would grant subloans based on its own appraisal of subborrowers'
investment plans, forwarding to IBRD for review only those plans for sub-
projects exceeding US$1.5 million equivalent. SBS would be responsible for
the organization and administration of technical studies under the project,
and the foreign exchange risk on this portion of the proposed loan would be
assumed by SBS.
vii.      IBRD loan funds (US$30.7 million) would be supplemented with about
US$14.8 million equivalent of SBS's funds from other sources for onlending
to subborrowers to cover 80% of the fixed investment costs of subborrowers'
projects. Subborrowers would be expected to contribute the remaining 20%.
The IBRD loan includes no provision for working capital, and SBS would be
required to provide funds of about US$3.6 million equivalent for this purpose.
IBRD loan funds of US$300,000 would cover the foreign exchange costs of for-
eign consultants or 50% of the costs of Yugoslavian consultants for the tech-
nical assistance studies; SBS would finance the remainder.



- iii -
*            viii.      SBS would charge 8.25% on loans to combinats and cooperatives for
their investment programs. To meet the seasonal credit requirements for these
subprojects, SBS would make available adequate short-term loans at 1% margin
over the estimated costs of borrowings which currently are about 8%.
ix.       The three Macedonian banks which merged to form SBS in January 1973
have never lent i I-aTrc to the private lE--m seth-ut always through com-
binats and cooperatives. This practice would continue under the project.
For private sector investment loans, SBS would charge combifnats and coopera-
tives 6.75% and these funds would be onlent at 8.25%. Seasonal credit for
the private sector would be made available to combinats and cooperatives by
SBS at estimated cost, and would be onlent with a margin of 1%. The current
seasonal credit rate to subborrowers therefore would be about 9%. An assured
spread of 1.5% on investment loans and 1% on seasonal loans, together with
100% refinancing and the benefits from economies generated by a greater mar-
keting and processing throughput, would provide combinats and cooperatives
sufficient incentives to assist private farmers with the preparation of their
investment plans and the necessary on-going technical advice.
x.        The combinats and cooperatives would be required to assume the for-
eign exchange risk for IBRD's portion of sub-loans in both the social and
private sectors. The terms of the subloans would be related to the cash flow
projections of the investments but would in no instance exceed 16 years in-
cluding a maximum grace period of five years.
S            xi.        The estimated financial rates of return to subprojects would range
from 12% to 45%. The rate of return to the economy is estimated at about
21%. The project is suitable for an IBRD loan of US$31 million for a term of
20 years including a grace period of five years.
.






YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia has
requested an IBRD loan to assist the expansion and modernization of agricul-
tural production and to construct facilities for processing agricultural prod-
ucts in the Republic of Macedonia.  The project would help finance subprojects
in both the social and the private sectors. In the social sector, integrated
agricultural industries enterprises--or combinats--would receive loans for the
expansion of their dairy and beef cattle and pig production; the construction
of a modern meat processing plant (with an annual production of about 11,000
tons of fresh and processed meats); the establishment of 2,600 ha of vine-
yards; the construction of wine-processing facilities to produce 42 million
liters of wine and carbonated drinks and 20,000 tons of wine by-products an-
nually; the construction of cold storage and grain silo facilities; and the
development of fish ponds to produce annually 1,500 tons of carp and 75 tons
of trout. Additionally, the social sector subprojects would provide expanded
markets for private farmers' production.
1.02      In the private sector, private farmers and farmers' cooperatives
(including tobacco enterprises) would receive loans for on-farm improvements,
including the construction of tobacco-drying sheds and the upgrading of cattle.
1.03      Although three previous IBRD loans included a small agricultural
component, this project would be the first for integrated agricultural de-
velopment in Yugoslavia. As of March 31, 1973, IBRD's total loans net of
cancellation amounted to US$660 million.
1.04      The project was prepared jointly by the Macedonian Government,
Stopanaka Bank and several agro-business combinats, and was assisted by an
IBRD preparation mission which visited Macedonia in April 1972.
1.05      This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission com-
posed of Messrs. E. Schertz, S. Bhatia and A. Stoneham (IBRD), and Messrs. A.
Brody, J. Daugherty, F. Hotes and H. Kordik (Consultants), which visited
Yugoslavia in July/August 1972. Additional contributions to this report were
made by Messrs. L. Helmers, R. Hanan and J. Bloom (IBRD).
II. BACKGROUND
A. General
2.91      The Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia covers some 256,000
km and comprises six republics and two autonomous provinces. The Republics



- 2-
of Bosnia and Herzegovina, Macedonia, Montenegro and the Province of Kosovo
are less developed than the Republics of Croatia, Slovenia, Serbia and the
Province of Vojvodina. The present population of the country is about 21
million and is increasing at about 1.0% per annum. Per capita Gross Material
Product (GMP) has been growing by about 9% annually since 1968, increasing
from US$447 to US$584 in 1971. 1/ Agriculture (including forestry and fish-
eries) is the second most important sector in the economy, contributing about
22% to GNP, after mining and manufacturing which contribute about 36%.
2.02      The regional distribution of per capita incomes is uneven.  The
average per capita GMP of the less developed regions was US$270 in 1968 and
has grown at about 8% annually to US$346 in 1971, whereas that of the more
developed regions was US$540 in 1968 and grew at about 9% annually to US$712
in 1971. The Federal Government is mindful of this regional economic dispari-
ty and has followed a balanced regional economic development policy with the
objective of encouraging growth in the less developed regions by transferring
resources from the more developed regions.
2.03      From 1966 to 1969, producers' prices were quite stable, growing at
about 1.5% per annum. Inflationary pressures, reflecting both cost push and
excess demand factors, resulted in an accelerating rate of price increase in
1970 and 1971 (10% and 15%, respectively). To curb this inflation, Govern-
ment embarked upon a stabilization program, beginning in late 1970, includ-
ing price control, a restrictive monetary policy and the development of an
incomes policy. In addition, the dinar was devalued twice during 1971 for
an overall decline of about 38% against its main trading partners. The re-
sults of these measures have been felt markedly in the external accounts.
Exports increased by 20% in 1972 while imports increased only by 6%; the
large current account deficit has been replaced by a substantial surplus
and foreign exchange reserves have trebled. There was also some slowing
down in the rate of inflation during 1972. With the implementation of the
nevly completed incomes policy, a further reduction of inflationary pressures
can be expected.
2.04      The Republic of Macedonia covers about 10% of the total land area
of Yugoslavia; it is situated in the southeastern part of the country and is
bordered by Greece, Bulgaria and Albania to the south, east and west, respec-
tively. The present population of about 1.7 million (about 8% of Yugoslavia's
total population) lives mainly in rural areas. Macedonia's population growth
rate (about 1.4% per annum) is above the national average.  The largest urban
1/   GMP is GNP less the value of non-productive services; the figures are
expressed in current prices and exchange rates.
0



3
center in the Republic is Skopje, which is also the administrative, cultural,
political and industrial center. Other large urban centers are Bitola, Titov
V'eles, Kumanovo and Prilep. Macedonia's per capita GMP of about US$380 char-
acterizes the Republic as one of the less developed regions in Yugoslavia. 2/
B. Macedonia's Agricultural Sector
2.05      Macedonia has basically an agricultural economy with about 47% of
its population employed in agriculture, which contributes about 28%T to its
CNP. Industry ranks second in importance, contributing about 23% to GNP.
Aggregate agricultural production has been growing more slowly than the
economy as a whole during the last several years. The main causes of this
slow rate of growth are inefficient agriculture and undeveloped industry,
in particular food processing. A detailed discussion of Macedonia's agri-
cultural sector and food processing industry is in Annexes 1 and 2.
2.06      In Macedonia, as in other parts of Yugoslavia, agricultural produc-
tion is organized into the social sector and private farms. The social sec-
tor consists mainly of large-scale, self-managed enterprises with vertical
integration of production, processing and marketing (combinats). They are
highly capital-intensive, utilizing modern production technology, and are
well-managed. Their main activities are in wheat and sugar production, to-
bacco marketing, grape and wine production and poultry and livestock products
processing. There are about 25 such combinats, and their present land hold-
ings account for about 20% of the total arable land and contribute about 30%
of Macedonia's total income from agriculture.
2.07      The private sector (private farmers and farmers' cooperatives) oc-
cupies the remaining 80% of Macedonia's arable land and accounts for the
remaining 70% of agricultural income. While the social sector dominates the
production of capital-intensive crops, the private sector concentrates mainly
on labor-intensive crops such as maize and rice, tobacco, vegetables, grapes
and other fruits and livestock products. There are about 160,000 private
farmers in Macedonia, 7,500 of whom are organized into about 170 farmers' co-
operatives. The maximum legal size of a private farm is 10 arable ha,
while the average holding is about 3 ha. Due to a lack of irrigation facil-
ities, agricultural input supplies and extension services, and marketing and
credit bottlenecks, production in the private sector has been low. During
the period 1966-1970, agricultural output from the social sector increased
at the rate of 8.4% per year, while that of the private sector grew at a
rate of only about 2.3%.
2/   For further information on the Yugoslav economy, see Current Economic
Position and Prospects of Yugoslavia, IBRD report No. EMA-48a, May 26,
1972.



-4-
2.08      The lack of irrigation facilities is one of the major factors limit-
ing agricultural production. The annual rainfall of 300 mm in crop production
areas is inadequate and arrives mainly in the colder months, thus necessitat-
ing irrigation for crops during the hot and dry summer months. Government
has recognized the importance of irrigation and serious efforts are being
made to meet this deficiency. With proper irrigation facilities, Macedonia
would be in an excellent position to exploit some of its natural advantages
regarding soils and climate (it has a Mediterranean as well as a continental
climate) to produce such crops as tobacco, rice, cotton, grapes and early
fruit and vegetables more competitively than the rest of Yugoslavia.
2.09      The social sector combinats and farmers' cooperatives procure their
inputs, such as fertilizers, seeds and machinery, from enterprises or directly
from imports. Private farmers procure these inputs mainly from their coopera-
tives or from combinats. In those cases where combinats have contracts with
private farmers for the production of vegetables, fruits or other industrial
crops, the farmers receive the required inputs from combinats. Although
Yugoslavia produces sufficient quantities of fertilizer, fertilizer consump-
tion in Macedonia in both social (40 kg per ha) and private (about 10 kg per
ha) sectors is low, due to the dry climate and lack of irrigation facilities.
Similarly, farm mechanization is still at a very low stage of development,
compared to other republics in the country. In 1969, there were about five
tractors per 1,000 ha, while the overall ratio for the country as a whole was
about seven per 1,000 ha.
2.10      The private farmers can receive technical advice from either com-
binats or cooperatives, but in practice this arrangement has proved only
partly effective. Within the past six months, an extension service has been
established by the Government of Macedonia to supplement the technical as-
sistance the private farmers are currently receiving from combinats and co-
operatives. Having been established with an initial staff of agronomists
and economists, the service aims to help farmers with on-farm development
programs and technical assistance.
2.11      The combinats and farmers' cooperatives either market their own
produce or sell it to those social sector combinats that have the capacity
to process it. Some of the combinats have their own arrangements for export
marketing, particularly in the case of processed fruits, vegetables and wine.
This has created numerous problems in export marketing, such as a lack of
quality and standardization control. In order to centralize and coordinate
export marketing, in particular of fruit and vegetables, the Federal Govern-
ment is establishing export agencies in major European markets.
2.12      Due to the smaller scale of production, private farmers must rely
on the processing capabilities of combinates and cooperatives for their in-
dustrial crops. Due to the lack of marketing information and other adminis-
trative arrangements, private farmers have to rely primarily upon coopera-
tives and combinats for the marketing of their produce. A small percentage



-5-
of their production, mainly vegetables, is sold in the open market where
prices may be higher than those paid by cooperatives and combinats.
C. Agricultural Credit in Macedonia
2.13      The major source of agricultural credit is the Stopanska Bank (SBS)
which acts as an economic development bank for Macedonia and conducts about
95% of all banking business. This bank was formed in January 1973 as the
result of a merger between the original Stopanska Bank, the Commercial and
Investment Bank - Skopje (CIBS) and the Commercial and Investment Bank -
Bitola (CIBB). A detailed discussion of these banks and the merger is given
in Annex 3.
2.14      No banks have lent directly to private farmers, who obtain their
finance through various forms of cooperation with combinats or cooperatives.
SBS has been willing to make credit available through combinats and coopera-
tives for private farm improvements provided specific programs had been drawn
up. Its main activity, however, has been to lend for combinat investment
programs.
2.15      The share of agriculture, including forestry and agro-industries,
in SBS's total lending has averaged 15% (about ND 400 million) over the past
three years. SBS has 11 departments and the Director of its Agriculture and
Forestry Loans Department (AFLD) is responsible for agricultural loans.  The
present technical staff in the department totals about 25 and are all located
in Skopje. SBS does not require security for loans made but relies upon its
own evaluation of each project. The number of bad debts has been insignificant.
2.16      As a result of the merger, SBS has assets of about ND 24.3 billion
(US$1.4 billion), annual profits of about ND 150 million (US$8.8 million), a
staff of about 1,250, and operates from some 27 offices, branches and sub-
branches. Profits are low when compared with international standards, but
normal for this type of operation in Yugoslavia. SBS brought to the merger
its investment expertise, particularly in industry and agriculture, while
CIBS brought its investment experience in tourism, commerce and building,
and CIBB its local investment and commercial experience in the Bitola area.
The merger is further discussed in paragraphs 4.01 through 4.03.
D. Macedonia's Development Plan
2.17      Under guidelines drawn up by the Federal Government for the eco-
nomic development of the republics, the 1971-1975 Social Plan leaves the
responsibility for plans and execution mainly to the republics. Accordingly,
local governments have to carry the responsibility for their own social en-
terprises, and are not prepared to keep uneconomic units alive. The Plan
0



- 6 -
specifically states that an end must be put to the practice of duplicating
manufacturing capacities in almost every republic without regard to economic
considerations.
2.18      This theme is also reflected in Macedonia's development plan, which
stresses the importance of modernizing production techniques and increasing
the competitiveness of social enterprises. The plan envisages investments
of about ND 3.2 billion (US$188 million) per annum, 58% of which are to be
channeled to the industrial sector, including agro-industries, and 15% to be
used for agricultural infrastructure development such as irrigation and land
reclamation.
2.19      For the first time on a large scale, funds are also to be made
available for the development of the private agricultural sector. The funds
- about ND 200 million (US$11.8 million) for the period 1971-1975 - are to be
administered by SBS to private farms through combinats and cooperatives for
new plantings, mechanization, livestock production, improvements, etc.
2.20      As part of the exercise of drawing up Macedonia's -lan, all the
agro-industries combinats submitted their investment proposals to Macedonia's
Ministry of Planning and the appraisal mission reviewed all the plans for
which consultant studirs had been prepared. Based on an analysis of markets,
existing capacities and financial and economic returns, the projects would be
feasible in the fields of livestock and meat processing, grape and wine pro-
duction and fishpond cultivation. Also, private farm development appeared to
be well worth pursuing and a first phase credit project was prepared. All
these projects are further discussed in Chapter III.
2.21      Several combinats had prepared projects in the field of fruit and
vegetable processing and edible oil extraction which would be beneficial to
the private sector. However, analysis showed that Yugoslavia has ample capa-
city to meet the present demand and that further studies r-Lirding raw mate-
rial availability, available processing capacity and market projections need
to be undertaken. The proposed project includes provision for such studies.
2.22      The Government, furthermore, expressed a keen interest in providing
infrastructure projects for the private farm sector. The possibility of in-
cluding an access road (US$10 million) and irrigation development program
(US$44 million) was discussed, but since the engineering designs had not been
completed and no analysis had been made of the benefits that would accrue to
private farmers, these projects could not be appraised. The proposed project
provides for completion of the engineering and economic studies.



-7-
III.  THE PROJECT
A. General Description
3.01      The project forms part of Macedonia's development program by pro-
viding loans over a five-year period to the social sector for modernizing
production methods and facilities, increasing the competitiveness of the
social enterprises and increasing exports. Credit also would be provided
under the project over the same period to the private farmers for tobacco
growing and drying, table grape production, dairy production and other on-
farm improvements. Technical assistance would be provided for studies to
improve private farm productivity.
3.02      The borrower of the proposed IBRD loan of US$31.0 million would
be SBS under guarantee of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia.
SBS's Agriculture and Forestry Loans Department (paras 2.15 and 4.04) would
make the technical appraisals. SBS would administer and finance the local
costs for foreign consultants and 50% of total costs of Yugoslav consultants
for the technical studies provided for in the project (para 3.23).
3.03      The components of the project are summarized as follows:
Number of   Subproject Cost       % Total

B. Detailed Features
Project Areas
3.04      The primary production components financed by the project - vineyard
establishment and livestock development - would be located throughout central
Macedonia (Map) in the irrigated major crop-producing areas. The processing,
storage and fishpond facilities would be constructed near the production areas
of central Macedonia where resources and access roads are readily available.
The private sector on-farm development components would be located throughout
Macedonia.
The Subborrowers
3.05      Under the proposed project, loans would be made available by SBS to
22 combinats for specific investment plans and to about 2,400 private farmers
through combinats and cooperatives for farm productivity impcovements. Each
agricultural industries combinat is an independent economic eaterprise in
accordance with the general Yugoslav concept of decentralized economic con-
trol and workers' self management of social propetties. The Workers' Coun-
cil, elected by the personnel of the enterprise, acts as the top policy-making
body in much the same fashion as the board of directors of a corporation in a
non-socialist country. The Workers' Council appoints the General Manager,
who is the chief executive officer of the enterprise.  In general, the com-
binats are well managed and operate efficiently.  Problems in accounts and
audit are discussed in paras 4.12 through 4.16.
3.06      Assurances would be obtained during negotiations that regarding in-
vestment in the social sector, only those combinats can participate in the
project for which the investment plans have been analyzed    this report and
that the proceeds of the IBRD funds on-lent to such combin3ts can be used
only for those subprojects. Annex 4 presents a list of participant combinats
and subprojects to be executed by each of them. If for any reason SBS would
reject any of the proposed subprojects, assurances were obtained that SBS
would inform IBRD of the reasons for rejection. The IBRD funds that became
available as a result of these rejections could be used for other investment
projects in the social or private sectors provided that social sector projects
benefit the private farm sector and provided SBS submits to IBRD for approval
a detailed feasibility and appraisal report.
3.07      Little is known of Macedonian private farmers' specific credit
demands and needs since banks have not been lending directly to farmers
leaving such lending to the combinats and cooperatives. Moreover, they do
not have the staff resources to appraise and administer a large volume of
private farmers' credit. The proposed private farmers' credit subproject
must therefore be regarded as a first phase proposal for SBS, combinats and
Government to gain experience with farmers and their problems. The long-term



loans under this subproject would be made to the private farmers through ap-
proved combinats and cooperatives and are expected to be used mainly for to-
bacco, table grapes, dairy and on-farm improvements. The appraisal and lend-
ing procedures are further discussed in Chapter IV.
Livestock
3.08      Subloans would be provided to agricultural combinats to finance
construction of buildings and other physical facilities and to purchase
equipment and machinery for five dairies, four dairy heifer rearing opera-
tions, four cattle fattening facilities and two commercial pig production
units. Subloans would also be provided to purchase the basic herd for dairy,
heifer rearing and pig operations. Also included would be provision for en-
gineering services for design and construction assistance and technical as-
sistance for training management and workers in dairy and liv.'estock produc-
tion technology. Construction of the livestock components of the project
would be phased over four years.
3.09      Dairy Farms.  Each of the five project farms would have a herd of
about 1,000 milk cows. The basic herd initially would consist of Holstein
or Friesian in-calf heifers, about 76% imported and 24% domestic. Semen
from government artificial insemination centers would be used exclusively.
Calves would be sold at three months of age to heifer rearing or cattle
fattening farms. At full capacity, in project year 4, the five dairy farms
would sell annually about 24 million liters of milk, about 2,375 bull calves,
500 cull heifer calves and 1,875 heifers.  In addition, about 1,000 cull cows
would be replaced by in-calf heifers to maintain full capacity.
3.10      Cattle Fattening Farms.  Each cattle fattening farm would have
capacity for feeding 1,000 young cattle. Feeder calves would be purchased
at about 110 kg and after feeding about nine months would be sold at about
435 kg. Bull calves and cull heifers would be purchased from project dairies
with outside local purchases being used to maintain operating volume at ca-
pacity. Feed would be largely concentrates with enough roughage to prevent
digestive troubles. Each year some 5,280 fattened cattle from the four farms
would be sold for slaughter.
3.11      Heifer Rearing Farms.  To effectively utilize the improved dairy
breeding stock produced on the dairy farms, four heifer rearing and breeding
farms would be established under the project. Selected heifer calves would
be purchased from the project dairies at three months and would be artifi-
cially inseminated at 15 to 16 months of age. At 23 months of age, the in-
calf heifers (due to calve in one to two months)-would be used as replace-
ments for the project dairies or sold to individual farmers to improve pro-
duction and quality in the private sector. Non-breeders, about 10%, would
be sold for slaughter. Feed would consist of silage, alfalfa and grain
mixed with protein and mineral supplements. About 2,160 bred heifers and
240 non-breeders would be available for sale annually from the four farms
after full development.
0



- 10 -
3.12      Pig Farms.  Under the project, two farms would be established to
produce about 10,000 and 30,000 slaughter pigs each at full development.
The total annual production of about 40,000 pigs would be equivalent to
about 3,000 tons of pork meat. The total breeding herd of the two farms -
over 2,000 sows farrowing 2.3 litters per year - would consist mainly of
Yorkshire, Landrace and crosses of these breeds. This intensive system would
employ modern methods of pig production including artificial insemination.
Mleat Processing Plant
3.13      A subloan would be provided under the project to construct a meat
slaughtering and processing facility near the center of the Xivestock pro-
duction area, making available a livestock market for the social and private
sectors. Included would be provisions for facilities and equipment with a
capacity to slaughter and process 15 cattle and 100 pigs per hour on a single
shift basis. The plant would be designed to meet the sanitary standards im-
posed by major importing countries including the EEC and USA. The various
processes would be designed for maximum efficiency, utilizing economic com-
binations of fully automated and semi-automatic equipment combined with mini-
mum handling and straight-line flow. Plant design would provide sufficient
flexibility to efficiently slaughter and process animals during the usual
seasonal variations in livestock supply. Included would be provision for
engineering services dvring construction and for technical assistance to
train staff in modern meat processing techniques.  At full production about
12,000 cattle and 125,000 pigs would be processed annually, -roducing about
11,000 tons of fresh, cured, and canned meat and about 3,2CQ -ons of by-
products (lard, bonemeal, etc.). Investment and construction would be over
a two-year period.
Vineyards
3.14      Subloans would be provided to 13 Macedonian combinats for new
planting of 1,600-ha vineyards for wine grapes and 1,000-ha vineyards for
table grapes. At full development, production of fresh table grapes would
be about 22,000 tons annually of which 17,600 tons would be sold fresh and
the balance - 4,400 tons - processed into wine. Grapes for wine production
would reach 24,000 tons. New plantings would be phased over a three-year
period with full production being reached in seven years.
3.15      Included in the project would be simple sheds for protecting ma-
chinery and table grape crates and concrete spray mixing basins. In addi-
tion, some all-weather access roads to and within the vineyards would be
constructed. Technical assistance would be provided to train employees
abroad in vineyard ebtablishment and wine production.



- 1 1 -
Wine Processing Facility
3.16      A subloan would be provided for the construction of a wine processing
facility for producing about 38 million liters of bulk and bottled red and white
wines, carbonated wines and brandy. Also included would be processing capac-
ity for producing about 4 million 1 of non-alcoholic carbonated beverages.
The plant's total annual output would be equivalent to about 40 million 1 of
high quality wine. Provision would be included for engineering services dur-
ing construction and for technical assistance for training of staff in wine
production techniques. About 41 million 1 of crude wine would be bought from
combinats and small farmers' wineries for blending, aging, and stabilizing
into a similar quantity of high quality wine.
Wine By-product Processing
3.17      A subloan would be provided for the construction of a new wine by-
product processing facility for processing annually about 10,000 tons marc
(the pressed cake left over from grape pressing) and 2,000 tons lees (the
residue after fermentation of wines). Also, a second wine by-product proc-
essing facility of similar size would be added to combinat Tikves's present
wine processing plant where construction would be limited to one building.
Each of the two combinats would obtain its inputs from its wine processing
plants and from other combinats.
3.18      Funds would also be available for engineering services for con-
S           struction and technical assistance for training of staff.  After develop-
ment, output from both plants is expected to reach 10,000 tons vinegar;
7,500 tons livestock feed; 1,500 tons grape seed for edible oil extraction;
740 tons alcohol wine distillate; 560 tons tartaric acid and wine acid and
100 tons baking powder, for a total of about 20,400 tons of by-products.
Cold Storge Facilities
3.19      Subloans would be provided for the construction of two 5,000-ton
capacity cold storage plants. The facilities would be single story, insulated
structures divided into chambers for storing fruit and vegetables, dairy, meat
and poultry products. Provision for engineering services during construction
are provided under the project.
Grain Storage Facilities
3.20      Subloans would be provided for the construction of a steel five-
cell corn storage complex (5,000 tons) and a concrete rice silo (2,500 tons).
Included would be provisions for materials handling equipment, electrical
system and miscellaneous equipment necessary for the efficient receipt,
storage, and discharge of grain. Provisions would be included for engineer-
ing services during construction.
0



- 12 -
Fish Ponds
3.21      Subloans would be provided for development of five carp fish ponds
and three trout fish ponds. Included in the fish pond component are provisions
for constructing water supply channels and ponds for breeding young fish, fish
fattening, and mother fish for trout fisheries and ponds for the carp fisheries.
There would also be provision for technical assistance to train employees in
fish fanming methods. At full developrment, 1,500 tons of carp and 75 tons
of trout would be produced per year.
Private Farmers' Credit
3.22      With an annual tobacco production of 24,000 tons, mostly Oriental
type, Macedonia accounts for almost 50% of Yugoslavia's total production.
Recent trends have shown an increasing demand for Virginia type tobacco, of
which about 5,000 tons were imported in 1971. It is Goverrment's policy to
support the substitution of these imports with increased domestic production.
At full development, the project would generate 1,400 tons of Virginia tobacco.
Credit would be provided to the private sector individuals and cooperatives
as well as tobacco enterprisesl/ to construct 150 tobacco drying sheds (13m
x 8.5m) with a drying capacity of about 10 tons per year of fluecured Virginia
tobacco. The single story sheds would be divided into utility area and a
drying chamber. Heat would be forced through sub-floor ducts from the furnace
to the dryers. Credit would also be provided for the development of table
grape vineyards on both non-irrigated and irrigated land from which yields
are expected to be about 13 tons and 20 tons per ha, respectively. Credit
would be made available for expanding dairy herds and for improving breeding
stock. This would result in both increased milk production and sales of
fattened cattle. Other on-farm improvements for increasing crop production
would also be financed.
Technical Studies
3.23      With the social sector having tha bulk of the processing and mar-
keting capability, in addition to its own raw material production, and the
private sector utilizing about 80% of all arable land, the sectors are largely
interdependent and complementary (paras 2.05-2.12).  The l&vcis of technology
and productivity in the private sectcr, however, lay well sehind, and in order
to promote balanced agricultural development the Government envisages a spe-
cial effort to assist the private sector. Three separate studies will be
undertaken with the general objectives of providing a clearer understanding
of private farm operations and examining the opportunities for improving £arm
productivity as well as the potential for fostering inproved cooperation be-
tween the private and social sectors. These studies are: (i) an irrigation
study the purpose of which would be to detarmine what is needed to finish the
partially completed irrigation projects in Prespa, Strumiitsa, Tikves and
Bregalnitsa and what benefits these systems would provide to the private
sector; (ii) a study
1/   Tobacco enterprises, located in each comwtune in 'Macedcfia, specialize
in the production of private farmers' tobacco, by providing inputs, tech-
nical assistance and marketing facilities.



- 13 -
sector; (ii) a study to determine the potential markets, domestic and export
for food products and the desirability of ex-anding Macedonia's food process-
ing industries, including the benefits they would provide to private farmers;
and (iii) a study of the benefits of the first phase private farmers' credit
program financed under this project and, based on this information, a feasi-
bility study for a second phase credit project. Detailed terms of reference
for these studies are given in Annex 5. They would be supervised by SBS and
would be undertaken by contractors. Assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions that these contractors would be appointed subject to IBRRD approval and
that contracts for the studies, also acceptable to IBRD, would be signed
within six months of signing of the Loan Agreement.

the fixed investment costs for the social sector and the tobacco dryers
credit. The 19% price contingency is based on a price increase of 5% per
year for imported materials and a gradually decreasing price increase from
12% to 6% per annum for domestically produced materials. The expected phas-
ing of project cost is shown in Annex 7.

Twenty percent of total project cost would be contributed by subborrowers and
30% from SBS.  The remaining 50% would be provided by the proposed IBRD loan
of US$31 million.
3.26      The IBRD loan would be made available directly to SBS and would
be used to finance 58% of project investment costs, excluding incremental
working capital, import duties and taxes. Under an agreement with the
Federal Government, the Republic of Macedonia would guarantee the provision
by SBS of counterpart funds for investments and technical studies under the
project, and to cover any cost overruns. Subborrowers would provide from
their own resources 20% of project investment costs including incremental
working capital (para 4.08). SBS would make available sufficient seasonal
and other short-term credit to meet the needs of all subprojects. These
financing arrangements were assured during negotiations.

E.  Procurement
3.27      International competitive bidding according to t-he Bank's guidelines,
and under the supervision of SBS, would be applied by the subborrower for the
procurement of the meat processing, wine processing and wine by-product process-
ing facilities. The processing plants would be procured under turnkey contract
since the facilities need to be integrated units. Specifications and tender
documents for the above subprojects would be submitted to the Bank for review
and approval before issue. Bids will be compared on the basis of c.i.f. prices
and domestic manufacturers will have a 15% margin of preference or the actual
duty whichever is less, in competing with foreign suppliers.  The bid awards
would also be subject to IBRD review and approval. The total contracts sub-
ject to international bidding would be about US$13.5 million (US$5.6 million
for the meat processing plant, US$4.0 million for the wine processing facil-
ities and US$3.9 for the two wine by-product processing facilities).
3.28      Procurement of in-calf dairy heifers according t_. -he Banks guidelines
for international competitive bidding would not be appropria;:e because the stock
needed to develop high production, basic herds are limited by breed and quality.
Due to limited domestic supply, about 75% of the heifers, equivalent to US$3.1
million would be obtained from abroad after receiving bid quotations from at
least three countries. The remainder, equivalent to US$0.8 million would be
procured locally. All purchases would be under the close supervision of the
SBS livestock and veterinary staff.  All purchase contracts between combinats
and suppliers would require the latter to ensure satisfactory arrangements for
delivery and be responsible for such cattle until received by project subbor-
rowers. SBS would submit to the Bank a consolidated summary showing the bids
received and selected together with a copy of the contract awarded to the
successful bidder.
3.29      International bidding would not be suitable for the other project
components. The facilities for the dairy, cattle fattening, heifer rearing
and pig farms are simple unintegTated structures consisting of many units,
widely dispersed throughout Macedonia, and could not be grouped into large
enough contracts to make international competitive bidding practicable. The
same dispersion is applicable to the vineyards where, moreover, the invest-
ment consists mainly of planting materials and trellises. Similarly, the
cold storage, silo, and fish pond subprojects are small and consist mainly
of civil works for which local contractors are more suitable. Finally, the
private farm credit components would not be suitable for international com-
petitive bidding since materials and equipment would be too diversified for
grouping. Procurement for these components would be through normal trade
channels and local bidding.  Subborrowers would follow SBSLs local bidding
procedures when the value of any subcontract exceeds US$20C000 equivalent.
SBS's procedures provide for solicitation of additional bids if less than
three are received initially and for evaluation of quality, delivery and
price.
3.30      The procurement of consultants for the technical assistance studies
would be in accordance with the Bank's guidelines for selection and use of
consultants.



- 17 -
3.31      During negotiations assurances were obtained that the procure-
ment procedures contained in paragraphs 3.27 through 3.30 would be followed.
F. Disbursement
3.32      Disbursement of the IBRD loan would be made against 67% of subloan
amounts paid out by SBS for approved fixed investments of combinats and private
farm development programs. Disbursement requests for social sector investments
would be supported by full documentation. For sub-loans to the private sector,
disbursements would be made against statements of expenditures, the supporting
documents and contracts for which would be retained by Stopanska Banka and
would be available for review during project supervision. IBRD loan funds of
US$300,000 would cover the foreign exchange cost of foreign consultants or 50%
of the costs of Yugoslavian consultants for the technical assistance studies.
IBRD would not disburse for incremental working capital loans or for seasonal
credits. Any savings would be reallocated to social or private sector
subborrowers who meet project criteria.
3.33      SBS's disbursements to subborrowers would take about 4-1/2 years and
disbursement of the IBRD loan to reimburse SBS is expected to take five years.
A schedule of estimated disbursements, by quarters, is given in Annex 8.
IV.  ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. The Proposed Lending Channel
4.01      As discussed in paras 2.13 to 2.16, the three major banks in
Macedonia merged in January 1973 to form the SBS. This is the proposed
lending channel for the project. The banks are more fully described in
Annex 3. IBRD was satisfied at loan negotiations that SBS's operational
policies have not changed substantially, particularly in respect of financial,
organizational, administrative and appraisal policies.
4.02      Furthermore, assurances were obtained during negotiations that
SBS's Agricultural and Forestry Loans Department (AFLD; para 2.15 and Annex
3) would continue to operate in SBS and that it would be responsible for all
loans under the project.
4.03      AFLD is headed by a Director and has a technical staff of 25.  There
are four main sections (see organigram attached to Annex 3): livestock;
production (mainly cereals, vegetables and fruits); processing (mainly food
and tobacco); and irrigation. There is also a small secretariat. AFLD staff
are well qualified, and loan appraisal and supervision procedures are very
good. Technical and financial analyses are thorough and realistic. Two addi-
tional senior staff would be required--an experienced senior food processing
engineer and a food marketing expert, familiar with domestic and export
markets--and their appointment would be a condition of loan effectiveness.



-18 -
B. Appraisal Procedures
4.04      SBS would make long-term loan funds available for combinat invest-
ment and private farm improvement programs. All social sector subprojects
have been analyzed in detail by IBRD. However, to take account of possible
changes in the economic and market environment as well as to assess the
creditworthiness of the subborrowers, SBS would appraise all social sector
subprojects. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that AFLD
would appraise subprojects as outlined in paragraphs 4.06 and 4.07.
4.05      SBS would forward to IBRD, for approval, investment plans for sub-
projects which exceed US$1.5 million equivalent or which have changed sub-
stantially since the time of IBRD appraisal. SBS would submit copies of
appraisals of all other social sector subprojects to the Bank. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations accordingly.
Social Sector
4.06      During negotiations, assurances were obtained that SBS would
ensure that:
(a)  subprojects in the social sector were technically feasible,
financially viable and commercially sound;
(b)  subprojects would generate sufficient cash flow to cover debt
service, reinvestment and satisfactory financial rates of return;            0
(c) for the meat, wine and wine by-products processing plants,
appropriate long-term supply contracts had been signed
(para. 5.04);
(d) adequate technical and financial supervision of on-going
subprojects would be provided; and,
(e) loan agreements with subborrowers would include clauses
satisfactory to IBRD covering insurance, inspection, man-
agement and training.
Private Sector
4.07      SBS would make project funds available to the private farm sector
through combinats and cooperatives and would refinance 100% of the amounts
loaned by the combinats and cooperatives to the farmers. During negotia-
tions, assurances were obtained that SBS:
(a) would satisfy itself that each of the participating com-
binats and cooperatives (i) would provide adequate and
continuing technical assistance to private farm subbor-
rowers, (ii) would make an adequate appraisal of each
request for a subloan and prior to approving such requests



- 19 -
would ensure that subprojects generate sufficient cash
flows to cover debt service and satisfactory financial
rates of return, (iii) would make available the neces-
sary seasonal credit each year to enable the full poten-
tial of the investment to be realized, and (iv) would
provide a suitable market outlet at fair and reasonable
prices for the bulk of the subborrowerst production,
preferably by a marketing contract; and
(b)  would make available to participating combinats and co-
operatives the necessary seasonal credit for onlending
to private farmers.
C. Lending Terms
4.08      During negotiations, assurances were obtained that subborrowers
both combinats and private farmers - would provide a minimum of 20% of the
investment cost, including at least 20% of their incremental working capital
requirements, from their own resources. Furthermore, assurances were
obtained that the combinats would be required to take the foreign exchange
risk on the IBRD portion of the subloans for their own investments; for pri-
vate farm improvement programs, the foreign exchange risk on the IBRD loan
portion would be borne by on-lending combinats and cooperatives.
4.09      Interest charged by SBS on investment loans varies at present from
3% for irrigation to 5.5% for general agriculture, and from 5% to 7% for
industry. Since 62.5% of investment funds would come from IBRD at 7.25%, 28.1%
from the Federal Fund for the Accelerated Development of Less Developed Republics
and Regions at 4.5% and 9.4% from SBS's own resources at a present average cost
of about 8%, the blend cost of funds to SBS would be about 6.5%. The following
interest rates would be applied by SBS to subborrowers for investment loans:
Social Sector   Private Sector
SBS to combinats and cooperatives for
their investments                                8.25%
SBS to comb/coop for relending to
private farmers                                   -                  6.75%
Comb/coop to farmers                                                   8.25%
Since the average cost of SBS's own funds may increase during the project's
disbursement period, an on-lending rate of 8.25% on loans to combinats and
cooperatives for their own investments should ensure a spread to SBS of at
least 1.5% to cover administrative, appraisal and other costs under the proj-
ect (Annex 3, Table 9). SBS would have only a nominal spread on loans to
combinats/cooperatives for private farm development programs (about 15% of



- 20 -
total project costs). This would be acceptable since SBS's administrative
costs would be negligible and the risk of subloan default and foreign ex-
change risk would not be carried by it. Combinats and cooperatives would
receive a spread of 1.5% for long-term funds on-lent to private farmers.
This would compensate them for administrative and technical assistance costs
as well as the foreign exchange risk. Since combinats and cooperatives are
presently unable to fully refinance loans to private farmers, the 100% re-
financing available under the project would provide a further incentive. In
addition to the 1.5% spread, the combinats and cooperatives would benefit
through economies generated by significantly greater throughput in their pro-
cessing and marketing operations. For social sector subprojects, SBS would
provide seasonal and other short-term loans, from their own resources, to
combinats at one percentage point over funds' costs. Initially, the estimated
rate to the subborrowers would be about 9%. The combinats and cooperatives
would obtain seasonal credit from SBS at the bank's cost of short-term funds
for on-lending to private farmers at one percentage point spread. During ne-
gotiations, assurances were obtained that SBS would apply the interest
rates exhibited above.
4.10      As discussed in Annex 3, interest on agricultural investment loans
in Macedonia is about 5.5% per annum. Thus, the rates proposed above would
result in an increase of 2.75% - an increase of 50% - which should be con-
sidered a first step toward a policy of more realistic interest rates. More-
over, the higher interest rates proposed, based on current blend cost of
funds, would permit elimination of interest subsidies which are currently
provided.  The trend of decreasing rate of inflation (para 2.03), coupled
with the full assumption of foreign exchange risk by subborrowers and Gov-
ernment's Intention to maintain a realistic exchange rate of the dinar, would
work towards positive interest rates for subborrowers.
4.11      The terms of the subloans under the proposed project would be
related to the cash flow projections of the investments (Annex 10). However,
assurances were obtained during negotiations that loans under the pro--

D.  Audit and Prolect Evaluation
SBS
4.12      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that SBS would
maintain a separate project account. This would include disbursements made
under the project for all subloans and for technical assistance.
4.13      The accounts of Yugoslav enterprises are not audited in the strict
sense of the term. Once a year, the Social Accounting Service verifies the
cash balances and conducts a limited check review of the financial statements
for mathematical accuracy and comparisons with previous years. A more de-
tailed field examination is conducted at irregular intervals of between one
and ten years. The main purpose of the Social Accounting Service is to ex-
amine whether financial transactions comply with Yugoslav law. The methods
used do not meet generally accepted auditing standards as the Service does
not verify the validity of any accounts. In order to have Bank loans audited
in accordance with sound auditing principles, the Government and the Bank
presently are discussing an appropriate training program under which Social
Accounting Service staff would be trained in auditing methods consistent with
Bank requirements. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that in the
event that the Social Accounting Service is not able to achieve a consistent
and satisfactory standard of auditing, the borrower would retain at the
request of the Bank a competent, experienced and independent auditor or
auditing firm, selected by the borrower after consultation with the Bank.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the borrower would submit
to the Bank certified financial statements in a format acceptable to the
Bank within three months from the end of each fiscal year. The borrower also
agreed to submit these financial statements auit~ed in accordance with sound
auditing principles to the Bank within sjx_-i-nths from the 'end of each fiscal
year together with appropriate explanatory notes.
Social Sector
4.14      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the parti-
cipating combinats would submit to SBS certified financial statements of
their enterprises within three months from the end of each fiscal year. The
borrower also agreed that such combinats would submit to SBS these financial
statements audited by the Social Accounting Service or another competent and
experienced independent auditor in accordance with sound auditing principles
within six months from the end of each fiscal year. These statements are
primarily intended for use in SBS's management accounting and control, to
allow SBS to analyze the effects of the combinats' investments under this
project on their profits. SBS would make these statements and analyses avail-
able to the Bank upon request.



- 22 -
4.15      The financial statements would be prepared in accordance with
Yugoslav laws. In general, such statements differ from those prepared in
non-socialist countries in the concept of ownership, priority of obligations
of an enterprise and disposition of net income. They are, however, recon-
ciliable with the format commonly used in non-socialist countries. Assur-
ances were obtained during negotiations that SBS would have such state-
ments converted to a format acceptable to IBRn upon request.
Private Sector
4.16      Under the technical assistance component of the main project,
funds would be provided for a contract with a university or consultant to
prepare a second phase private farmers' credit project (para 3.23).  Under
the terms of the contract, the study would include an analysis of the effect
of the proposed credit scheme on the private farm sector. Detailed terms of
reference are in Annex 5. During negotiations, assurances were obtained
that a detailed eva uation study would be_ent to the Bank within four months
of the end of each calendar year effective for-calendar year 1974.

In addition, the heifer rearing units would produce annually about 2,100
heifers which would be sold for breed improvement to project dairy combinat
farms or other farms. Furthermore, the wine by-product processing facilities
would produce about 20,000 tons of by-products, mainly vinegar and livestock
feed.
5.02      The processing units in the social sector (the meat processing plant
and the wine and wine by-product processing plants) would depend for their
raw material supplies on the project combinats as well as on other combinats
and the private sector. The total annual input of the meat processing plant
would be about 12,000 head of cattle and 125,000 pigs (equivalent to an out-
put of about 11,000 tons of processed meat) and therefore it would need to
buy from non-project sources about 6,500 head of cattle (about two-thirds
from other combinats and one-third from the private sector), and about 85,000
pigs (primarily from other combinats). As discussed in Annex 9, combinats in
Macedonia have substantially expanded their cattle and pig farms while the
private sector has always had difficulty in marketing its cattle and pigs;
thus, the meat processing plant would provide an outlet for the farm produc-
tion and should have no problem procuring the required numbers of livestock.
5.03       Similarly, the wine processing complex would need to buy about 21
million 1 of crude wine from other sources (about 13 million 1 from other
combinats' present production and about 8 million 1 from the private sector's
present production) for its annual output of about 40 million 1 wine equiva-
lent. The two wine by-product processing plants would require about 20,000
tons of marc and 4,000 tons of lees which would be procured from their own
wineries (50%) and from other combinats (50%). As the bulk of Macedonia's
wine production is in crude form which needs processing, the wine and wine
by-products processing plants would be adequately supplied.
5.04      Moreover, assurances were obtained during negotiations that SBS
would approve the subloans for the meat, wine and wine by-products processing
plants only if long-term phased delivery contracts had been signed by supply-
ing combinats to deliver annually by 1976, sufficient raw material to meet
minimum economic operating requirements.
5.05      It is estimated that more than 2,400 private farmers would make
use of the credit facilities, mainly for tobacco growing and drying, table
grape production and dairy production. Their incremental production is
estimated at about 1,400 tons of tobacco, 2,800 tons of table grapes and
some 4 million 1 of milk.
B. Markets and Prices
5.06      Most of the incremental production of the commodities financed
under the project would be marketed domestically. Yugoslavia's GNP is ex-
pected to continue to grow at a substantial rate. The annual influx of
tourists is expected to double within the next ten years. The major outlet
0



- 24 -
0
for Yugoslavia's agricultural production definitely appears to be the domes-
tic market. A detailed discussion of the market aspects of each project com-
modity is in Annex 9.
5.07      Although the bulk of the production ftrom subprojects would be mar-
keted domestically, prices would be competitive in world markets. Under the
project, it is expected that some small quantities of meat (about 2,000 tons),
table grapes (about 2,000 tons) and wine (about 2 to 2.5 million 1) could be
exported. The major market for fresh meat would be Italy, for canned meat
the USA and Federal Republic of Germany (FRG), for table grapes Austria and
FRG for wines the U.S., Austria and FRG (see Annex 9).
5.08      In the last five years, prices of meat and meat oroducts on the
world markets have shown a significant increase and this trend is expected
to continue in view of the world meat shortage. However, for the financial
analysis, the 1972 prices have been used in order to calculate the returns
conservatively.  Regarding the prices of the other commdodities, domestic
prices in Yugoslavia increased significantly between 1969 and early 1971,
but were stabilized due to Governmentts partial price freeze and are now iyI
line with world market prices. It is Government's policy to maintain this
competitive position. Although international prices are expected to increase
slightly in the future, the financial calculAtions have again been based con-
servatively on 1972 domestic and export prices, as discussed in Annexes 9 and
10.
C. Operating Results
5.09      Financial models for typical investments under the project are given
in Annex 10. The financial rates of return of the 17 models vary between 12%
and 45%. Also a sensitivity analysis was made: if investment costs were in-
creased by 10% the financial rates of return would vary from 11% to 43%; if
net income were reduced by 10%, the financial rates of return would fall to
between 10% and 42%.
5.10      A cash flow projection for SBS's participation in the project is
given in Annex 11. The projections show that subborrowers' repayments would
generate sufficient inflow to repay the IBRD loan within 20 years including
5 years grace and to cover a reasonable degree of slippage in repayments.
An,; surplus funds from loan repayments not required to service IBRD debt
would be relent on the same terms to subborrowers, who meet project criteria,
in the agricultural sector.
VI. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
6.01      About 20% of Macedonia's working population is at present either
unemployed or underemployed, and it is estimated that the social sector would
hire under the project about 1,150 additional workers.  It can be realistical-
ly assumed that the unemployed labor force would be correspondingly reduced.                _



*                                           - 25 -
Since, without the project, unemployed workers would have to depend on social
insurance benefits of about ND 1,700 per annum, the direct cost to the economy
without the project would have been about ND) 2 million per annum. The reduc-
tion in such benefits is, then, a surrogate for the value of the incremental
real production of these workers.  Taking into account this additional bene-
fit of the project the economic rate of return would be about 21%. The eco-
nomic rates of return of the prop3sed investments are higher than the finan-
cial rates of return mentioned in paragraph 5.09 because import duties and
taxes on investments are not censidered in the calculation and also because
the project would generate additional, mainly employment, benefits. Details
are presented in Annex 12.
6.02      The annual net incomes of the 2,400 private farmers participating
in the project xwould increase on average from ND 5,270 (US$310) to about
ND 7,395 (US$435).
6.03      As has been mentioned, the incremental production under the project
would be sold mainly in the domestic market.  I-However, some quantities would
be exported (para 5.07) and the foreign exchange earnings from these exports
would cover slightly more than the debt service (amortization and interest)
of the proposed Bank loan. Also, of course, a substantial quantity of project
production would be purchased by tourists. However, no attempt was made to
estimate what these indirect foreign exchange earnings would be.
6.04      With a high economic rate of return, and significant employment and
income distribution effects in the small farm sector, the project deserves
the high priority given to it by the Government of Macedonia and is suitable
for an IBRD loan of US$31 million, for a term of 20 years including a grace
period of five years.

follow the appraisal procedures specified in paragraph 4.07;
(e)  subborrowers would provide a minimum of 20% of the
fixed investment cost including at least 20% of their
incremental working capital requirements from their own
resources, and combinats and cooperatives would be

Stopanska Bank, for a term of 20 years, including a five-year grace period.
