YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
Macedonia's Agricultural Sector
Climate and Soils
1.        Macedonia, the southernmost Republic of the Yugoslavian Federation,
stretches from 40° to 43° northern latitude and 21° to 230 eastern longitude.
It is bordered by the Yugoslav Republic of Serbia and the autonomous region
Kosovo on the north, Albania on the west, Bulgaria on the east and Greece on
the south. The total land area is about 25,700 km2.
2.        The country is mountainous with elevations up to about 2,800 m
above sea level. Agricultural production is concentrated mainly in fertile
river valleys and plains. The three dominant mountain ranges run north to
south. While two of these ranges, the eastern and central mountain chains,
consist mainly of granite and volcanic rock formations, the western part along
the Albanian border is made up of dolomites and marble.
3.        In the valleys and plains alluvial soils are piedoninant.  Delluvial
and terra rossa soils are common to the foothills. Soil erosion is a major
problem on steep, barren hills and mountain slopes. Cultivated soils are
usually well supplied with organic matter and potash, but poor in phosphates.
The pH ranges from acid to neutral in the central and eastern part of the
country, while alkaline soils are located in the western part.
4.        Climatically, two regions can be distinguished.  The eastern and
central part has a Mediterranean climate, which is characterized by relatively
mild winters and hot summers. Most of the rain falls in the autumn and win-
ter months while summers are dry. Temperatures range from -4°C to 37°C and
average rainfall is 500 mm. The northern and western parts of Macedonia have
a continental climate with hot summers and cold winters. Rain falls mainly
during the winter and spring months. Temperatures go as low as -14°C in
winter and up to 40QC in summer. Average rainfall is about 650 mm with
western mountain areas receiving up to 730 mm. Late and early frosts are
a common hazard to both climatic zones and so are hail storms.
Agriculture's Contribution to GNP
5.        In 1971, about 800,000 people or 47% of the total population of
1.728 million were employed in the agricultural sector. The high proportion
of agricultural population is partly due to the underdevelopment of the
industrial sector and the lack of alternative employment opportunities.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
6.        Macedonia's GNP amounted in 1971 to ND 9.538 billion (US$560 million),
to which agriculture and forestry contributed ND 2.675 billion (US$156 million)
or 28.1%. During the 1966-1970 period, the economy grew at a rate of 4.5%
annually, while the agricultural sector's growth amounted to 3.8% per annum.
Within the agricultural sector, development has varied. The largest growth
has been accomplished in fruit and wine production (10.5% annually), while
growth rates of traditional crops have been less impressive (1.4% annually).
Crop farming is still more important than livestock farming, with farmers on
average getting 55% of their cash incomes from crop sales and only 45% from
sales of livestock products.
Organizatioi of the Agricultural Sector
7.        Macedonia's land tenure system is characterized by 160,000 private
farms. About 7,500 farmers belong to farmers' cooperatives of wnich there
are about 170. Due to legal restrictions limiting maximum size of private
farms to 10 ha of arable land and traditional inheritance customs (splitting
farm units equally among children), the average holding is only 3.0 ha.
The total arable land held by private farms is about 470,000 ha or about 80%
of the overall total.
8.        The social sector consists mainly of agro-business enterprises
(combinats), of which Macedonia has 25, usually consisting of organizations
with vertical integration of production and processing.  Their industries
deal especially with wheat, sugar and wine processing. Combinat land - about
20% of the total arable land in Macedonia - usually has been acquired through
reclamation of swamps and flooded areas, expropriation of estates from big
landowners and lots bought from private farms. Due to efficient management,
the social sector's annual growth of output amounted to 8.4% during the
1966-1970 period. The private sector's growth during the same period reached
only 2.3% per annum.
Land Use
9.        Out of Macedonia's total area of 2.571 million ha, 1.367 million
ha or 53% are classified agricultural lands. The remainder is forest and
waste land. About 50% of the agricultural lands is permanent pastures
(689,000 ha), about 43% is arable (583,000 ha) and the rest is orchards,
vineyards and meadows (see Table 1).
10.       About 53% of the arable land is devoted to cereal production
(305,000 ha), followed by industrial crops (65,000 ha), vegetables (51,000
ha) and forage crops (29,000 ha). Vineyards have been planted on about
26,000 ha and orchards on 20,000 ha.  During the past 10 years, cereal
hectarage decreased by more than 18%, while hectarage devoted to industrial
crops and vegetables increased by 16% and 46% respectively. Permanent pas-
tures and meadows/leys decreased during the same period by about 16%.
.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Agricultural Crops
11.       Average crop yields are low (see Table 2), mainly due to insufficient
rainfall.  Average yields of the private sector are generaLly below those ob-
tained by the social sector, mainly due to a lack of technical experience and
limited use of modern inputs. This difference becomes most apparent in irri-
gated agriculture where the social sector is obtaining yields which compare
favorably with other countries while the private sector yields are up to 50%
lower.
12.       Macedonia produces a variety of agricultural crops.  Its most
important cereals (total annual production about 560,000 tons) are wheat,
maize and barley. In volume, the next most important crop is vegetables
with an annual production of about 440,000 tons. Industrial crops, such as
sugarbeets, tobacco and cotton account for about 219,000 tons annually.
Annual grape production is about 182,000 tons followed by other fruits with
112,000 tons.
13.       Wheat, barley and maize account for the bulk of cereal production.
Although hectarage has been reduced from the 1961-65 average of 256,000 ha
to 237,000 ha in 1970, production increased from 399,000 tons (1961-65 average)
to 478,000 tons in 1970. Average yields are still lower in Macedonia than for
the country as a whole (Macedonia 1.87 tons/ha, Yugoslavia 2.15 tons/ha), but
production is growing at a faster rate than in other parts of Yugoslavia.
14.       Production of vegetables, both fresh and for processing, is of great
importance to the private agricultural sector. In 1970 production reached
134,000 tons melons, 91,000 tons tomatoes, 82,000 tons potatoes, 80,000 tons
peppers and 44,000 tons onions. Macedonia accounts for about 30% of Yugos-
lavia's tomato and green pepper production. Other vegetables produced include
peas, beans, cucumbers, cabbages, leeks and eggplants. Despite favorable cli-
matic conditions, Macedonia has only a two to three week advantage in early
vegetable and fruit production over its competitors within the country. No
apparent advantages exist in regard to international competition from coun-
tries like Spain, Italy, Greece, Turkey and Bulgaria.
15.       All the industrial crops (sugarbeets, tobacco, sunflower and cotton)
are processed and marketed by the combinats, although most of these crops ex-
cept sugarbeet are grown by the private sector.
16.       Sugarbeet production started in Macedonia only in 1959 with the
installation of a sugar mill at Bitola. Hectarage under sunarbeet is about
4,400 ha, mainly farmed by combinats. Total sugarbeet output reached 165,000
tons in 1970 with an average sugar content of almost 20%. Due to concentrat-
ing production on the combinats' irrigated lands, average yields of about
37.7 tons/ha are the highest in the country.
17.       Macedonia grows about 50% of Yugoslavia's total production of
oriental tobacco and about 70% of production is exported. In 1970 about



ANNEX 1
Page 4
29,000 ha were under tobacco and total production was about 24,000 tons
dried leaf.  Production is primarily in the hands of private farmers who
sell to the social sector for processing. Rowever, in recent years, trends
in world demand for oriental tobacco have shown indications of a decline.
As a result, the planners in Macedonia are eneouraging the production of
Virginia-type over oriental types in those areas where water is available.
However, Virginia tobacco requires mechanical drying, while oriental tobacco
is, at present, mainly sun-dried. Since tobacco growing is the main occu-
pation of a large number of private farmers in Macedonia, efforts must be
made (by the provision of drying units, etc.) to revitalize and reorganize
the industry to meet world trade and domestic demands. The prices of botn
Virginia and oriental tobacco have remained stable in recent years.
18.       Due to low producer prices during the past years, sunflower pro-
duction was limited to about 14,000 ha in 1970 and the total output of sun-
flower seed produced was about 16,000 tons, with the social and the private
sectors each producing about equal quantities. A drastic producer price
increase (44%) announced in early 1972 will offer an adequate incentive to
hectarage expansion.  Due to the price increase, net returns on sunflower
production will improve and make it a more interesting crop than cereals,
especially maize.
19.       Macedonia's cotton production reached about 12,000 tons of seed
cotton in 1970 but yields are low because of climatic conditions. Macedonia
represents the most northern part of the cotton growing area in the world
and conditions are not favorable. The medium staple fiber is produced and
it finds a ready market in the country.
20.       Grapes are another important agricultural commodity.  Production,
both in terms of area and yields, increased about 8Z per year between 1969
and 1971.  In 1971 about 174,000 tons of grapes were marketed, of which about
one-third were sold as table grapes.  At present, the annua-l per capita con-
sumption of table grapes in Yugoslavia is about 12 kg. The major outlet for
table grapes is the domestic market and only about 3-5% of total production
has been exported in recent years, mainly to Austria and West Germany. The
major producing season is from late July to October, which coincides with that
of other major table grape - producing European countries. However, for
climatic reasons, the table grapes produced in Macedonia are of an excellent
quality and taste and Macedonia has the potential of increasing its share of
exports to Europe. Both domestic and export prices of table grapes have in
- cent years recorded slight increases and this trend is expected to continue
in the future. Macedonia has the potential of increasing the production of
table grapes both from the market and supply points of view and hence efforts
should be made to increase production in the future.
21.       In 1971, about 127,000 tons of wine grapes were produced by the
social sector (50,000 tons) and the private sector (77,000 tons) from which
about 60 million 1 of wine were made. Wine quality of a substantial part
of the production is not satisfactory, mainly because of a lack of blending
and storage facilities.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
22.       Among fruits produced, apples are the most important with 49,800
tons picked in 1970 followed by plum-prunes 30,100 tons, pears 12,900 tons,
peaches 5,400 tons and apricots 4,800 tons. Other fruits produced locally in-
clude cherries, sour cherries and quince. Production is mainly in the hands
of private farmers although combinats are now establishing apple and pear
plantations on a large scale.
Livestock
23.       As a result of the rising standard of living, the consumption of
beef in Yugoslavia of about 8.1 kg per person is expected to increase.
Similarly, the world demand for meat is also rising and in recent years there
has been a shortage of meat leading to a rise in international prices.
24.       In Macedonia, total beef production in 1971 was about 5,000 tons.
The per capita consumption of fresh beef was about 4.7 kg which was well below
the national average. Similarly Macedonia was also below tae national average
in per capita consumption of pork meat and other fresh and processed meats.
However, as projected in Macedonia's present five-year plan, the per capita
consumption of both meat and dairy products is expected to increase signi-
ficantly (an income elasticity of 0.85 for meat is calculated) and therefore
a greater emphasis has been placed on increasing the production of both meat
*        and dairy products to meet increased domestic and export demands.
25.       In 1971 there were about 288,000 head of cattle, mostly for milk
production, of which some 272,000 belonged to private farmers. However, the
social sector, with better breeding stock and improved technology, had a
much higher production yield (about 4,000 kg milk per cow) than the private
farmers (about 1,200 kg per cow) and is now expanding its herds rapidly.
In order to improve quality and increase production, a govern.ment - subsidized
artificial insemination service is now available to cow owners.
26.       Between 1966 and 1970, pig production in Macedonia varied between
101,000 and 83,000 head, which is normal for this industry. The 1971 in-
ventory was 106,000 head with about 78% of these in the private sector.
However, six combinates have already established new pig farms and it is
expected that production will double within the next few years. The 1970
production of pork was 5,000 tons.
27.       In Yugoslavia, sheep numbers have been declining since 1968, with
Macedonia showing a similar trend. In 1970, Macedonia had 1,863,000 sheep.
- about 21% of the Yugoslav sheep population. Sheep milk production was
39,000 tons and production of lamb and mutton was 8,000 tons. About 81% of
Macedonian sheep are owned by individual farmers.
28.       Large units, requiring a high degree of technical skill, charac-
terize the vertically integrated poultry operations of the combinats. Pro-
duction per hen is much above the farm flock in the private sector.  The
_        social sector has increased numbers from 262,000 to 638,000 between 1965 to
1970 and is continuing to expand, while private farmers have reduced poultry
numbers from 1,828,000 to 1,498,000. Production in Macedonia reached 216
million eggs and 2,000 tons of poultry meat in 1970.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
Fish Production
29.       The present production of fish in Macedonia is 911 tons (mainly
carp), which does not cover its consumption of about 3,900 tons. The present
per capita consumption of 2.3 kg is expected to rise to 2.7 kg by 1975. It
appears that an increase in production will be necessary in order to meet
increased levels of demand both in Macedonia and in other parts of the country.
Macedonia has suitable climatic conditions for the production of fresh water
fish and technology has already been developed which would permit further
increases in production without difficulty.
Marketing and Pricing
30.       Agricultural products in Macedonia are marketed through several
channels depending upon availability and pricing. The private sector may
sell its production directly to village or urban markets, either individually
or collectively. Alternatively, they may sell to cooperatives and combinats
who may process the primary products or market them fresh through their own
sales organization or other marketing enterprises as they do with their own
production. Some combinats and cooperatives have their own wholesale-retail
distribution systems (cold storage, warehouses, transport and retail outlets)
which may cover other republics and provinces. For export of some commodities,
the Federal and Republican Governments provide an organizational framework
to coordinate and control marketing.  Social sector enterprises in some cases            S
have their representatives in major export markets.
31.       Prices for agricultural products are determined by a mix of market
forces and governmental controls. These partial controls include: i) guar-
anteed support prices on, for example, wheat and rice; ii) minimum sale
prices for sunflower seeds, sugar beets, etc.; iii) ceiling prices on bread,
milk and sugar; and iv) subsidies of about 15X in certain circumstances on,
e.g., milk. Despite the above, many of these controls have no real effect,
as commodities are above support or minimum prices. Moreover, the trend has
been away from price controls and towards a free market; meat, for example,
has been completely de-controlled. Consumer prices are, for the great ma-
jority of products, set freely in the market, with reference to international
prices as well.
Irrigation
32.       In 1972 about 60,000 ha were under irrigation, as indicated in
the table below which summarizes existing and proposed irrigation project
areas:

33.       Surface irrigation is practiced on about 50,000 ha, while sprinkler
irrigation systems are used on about 10,000 ha of the presently irrigated area.
Construction of new or extended irrigation systems is underway on 49,200 ha.
Financing for these works has been arranged by the agencies concerned and the
Republic of Macedonia. Engineering studies have been completed on another
114,000 ha and it has been estimated by Macedonian consulting engineers in
the irrigation field that approximately 117,500 ha in addition to that men-
tioned already, could be irrigated.
34.       Eighty-eight percent of the presently-irrigated land (and 90% of
the total estimated irrigable land) is within the Vardar River Basin. The
Vardar drains southward into Greece and thence to the Aegean Sea. It is
known as the Axios River in Greece. Slightly over 6% of the land irrigated
now is in the drainage area of the Strumeshitsa River, which flows into
Bulgaria and thence to Greece and the Aegean Sea. Another 3% is tributary
to the Crni Drim, which drains north and west into the Adriatic Sea via
Albania, and the remaining 3% of the land irrigated is in the Lake Prespa
Basin which is landlocked with no surface outlet to any ocean or sea.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
35.       The importance of the Vardar-Axios River Basin to both Yugoslavia
and Greece has caused the establishment by UNDP of a special study team
working in Macedonia and Greece to develop recommendations for an overall
master development plan for the stream system. Hydroelectric power, domestic
and industrial water supply and irrigation, and other water uses will be
considered. This study will be completed by 1976.
Supporting Services
36.       The social sector usually purchases all input requirements from
state enterprises or imports directly. The private sector can buy its re-
quirements such as seeds, fertilizer, pesticides and machinery at the exist-
ing 150 cooperatives or receive them from combinats or other state enter-
prises. Combinats having contracts with farmers for the production of veg-
etables, fruits, or industrial crops, usually supply services with required
inputs under the terms of the agreement.
37.       The combinats produce certified seed of good quality.  Part of the
vegetable seeds and vines for planting are imported, mainly from the USA,
the Netherlands and Italy. Fertilizer consumption is low due to small yield
responses as a consequence of the dry climate. While Yugoslavia's social
sector as a whole applied about 110 kg nutrients per ha in 1969, Macedonia's
combinats used only 40 kg per ha, with the bulk going to irrigated lands.
Private farms applied only 10 kg nutrients per ha in 1969.  All fertilizer
is produced in Yugoslavia. Use of pesticides is common practice in both
sectors, especially for protecting vineyards.
38.       Farm mechanization is still at a lower level in Macedonia than in
other republics of the Federation. In 1969 there were 5.3 tractors per
1,000 ha in Macedonia compared with 6.7 tractors per 1,000 ha for the country
as a whole. The use of combines has increased and in 1969 there were 5.3
combines per 1,000 ha in Macedonia compared to 8.0 per 1,000 ha as national
average.
39.       Contracting services to private farmers are provided by combinats
and cooperatives. Some private farmers have also gone into contracting
services, thereby making best use of equipment which would otherwise be too
expensive for their own small holdings.
40.       Until about six months ago, there were no Govern.2nt extension
services available in Macedonia. Combinats and cooperatives employ large
numbers of university graduates, agronomists and technicians who keep in
close contact with the agricultural university at Skopje and the various
research institutes, but this assistance to private farmers has not been
significant. The private sector, lacking adequate technical assistance,
is therefore still following outdated cultivation or livestock raising
techniques and is making little use of modern inputs.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
41.       The importance of agricultural research for the further development
of the sector has been recognized by Government, which is supporting a num-
ber of highly specialized institutes. Work on basic research has been
assigned to the agricultural university at Skopje, while applied research
is handled by autonomous specialized institutes. The Agricultural Research
Institute, Skopje, with a substation at Bitola covers agronomic aspects of
cereal and vegetable production. Other research organizations are the Wine
Research Institute, engaged in vineyard and wine production research; the
Rice Station, Kocani; the Tobacco Institute, Prilep; the Cotton Institute,
Strumica; the Vegetable Oil Section, Titov Veles; the Fruit Research Institute;
and other separate organizations investigating problems of livestock produc-
tion and aspects of irrigated agriculture. Applied research work is mainly
oriented towards adaptation and breeding of new high yielding varieties.
Development of new improved cultivation techniques as well as fertilizer and
plant protection trials are given high priority. All institutes are well
equipped and staffed with qualified researchers.

AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
The Food Processing Industry
A. Yugoslavia
1.        In Yugoslavia, as in many countries, accurate data on food processing
are unavailable. National statistics are not recorded for home and farm-pro-
duced foods, except for animals slaughtered for meat (Table 1). Large amounts
of fresh fruit and vegetables which have undergone only minimal classification
and cleaning move through private market channels. It has been estimated that
about half of all agricultural output is marketed through organized channels
and is therefore recorded in national statistics.
2.        Volume of Processed Food Production.  Data on processed food pro-
duction, from both industrial and reported private sector sources (Table 2),
indicate increases from 1968 to 1970 in most items that may be categorized
*       as having a relatively high value added.  They include:  bread and pastry,
biscuits and cookies, canned vegetables, other vegetable preparations, semi-
prepared and prepared foods, fresh meat, bacon and sausages, canned meat,
canned fish, margarine, edible oil, and alcoholic beverages. At the same
time, declining production was recorded for items exhibiting a lower value
added, including: rice, various fruit preparations, concer-rated soup, edible
animal fats, smoked and salted cured fish, butter, sugar and candy. Federal
Government data on the growth of Yugoslavia's food industry, using an index
with 1969 physical volume = 100, show 1968 = 92 and 1970 =111 (Table 3).
3.        Value of Processed Food Production.  The total value of processed
foods increased by over 50% between 1967 and 1970, when it was calculated at
over 22,000 million dinars (US$1.3 billion). This figure, however, includes
double accounting since the data for many raw materials such as flour and
vegetable oil appear again in the finished product. The greatest gains in
value (Table 4) were shown by: milk and eggs, preserved fruit and vege-
tables, preserved meats, meats, and the category "other food industries"
(e.g. yeast, coffee, glucose).  Within these categories, significant increases
were scored for fresh pork, lamb and mutton, bacon, canned meats, fruit syrup,
natural fruit juice, canned vegetables, dry baked goods and alcoholic bev-
erages. Except for fresh meat, these products again represent a relatively
high value added.
4.        Exports and Imports.  Exports of Yugoslavian processed foods are
significantly higher than imports, both in quantitative and value terms.
In 1970, meat exports totalled 127,000 tons (of which fresh meat 71,200
tons and canned meats 25,400 tons), processed fruit 44,200 m tons and
processed vegetables 12,500 m tons. Due to domestic shortages, however,
fresh meat exports were sharply curtailed in 1972. The major destinations



ANNEX 2
Page 2
of processed meat in 1970 were Italy and the Federal Republic of Germany,
with the United States taking a significant quantity of canned pork and
canned beef. Export data indicate a general diversity of small exports for
the other food products rather than a concentration on a limited number of
products exported in large quantities.
5.        Imports of processed fruits and vegetables in 1970 were about
13,000 m tons, primarily products such as fruit salad, pineapple, canned
tomatoes, pickled cucumbers and sauerkraut. Only 62 m tons of canned meats
and 440 m tons of sausage and other cured meats were imported.
6.        Processing Plants.  In 1970, there were in the social sector about
230 industrial enterprises which produced almost 900 million m tons of food
and 665,000 1 of beer for a total turnover of some 14,600 million dinars
(US$860 million). Among these social industrial enterprises were 77 abattoirs,
42 fruit and vegetable processing plants, 22 edible oil factories, 13 sugar
refineries and 28 breweries.
7.        Additionally, Yugoslavia has four metal can-making factories, none
of which are located in Macedonia, and three glass jar and hottlemaking facili-
ties, one of which is in Macedonia. The gross capacity of rijth industries
is sufficient to supply present and projected needs in the country. There
are also factories that manufacture laminations for flexible and semi-rigid
plastic, paper/plastic, paper/aluminum and foil/plastic packaging of thermally
stabilized food products.
8.        Meat Processing.  Over half the 18 million cattle, pigs and sheep
slaughtered in 1971 were slaughtered privately. The remainder were slaughtered
in about 1,700 abattoirs of which about 90 are industrial slaughterhouses,
about 400 small plants operated by enterprises and cooperatives and about
1,200 small private butcher shops. Lack of sanitary facilities, sufficient
space and adequate cooling capacity is a major problem throughout the industry,
particularly in the smaller plants. About 60 facilities are licensed for
export of one or more items.  Only eight have U.S. Departnent of Agriculture
approval, for some meats, and 31 have approval for European :.Larkets.
9.        Utilization varies from 50% to 90% with most plants operacing in
the range of 60% to 70% capacity on a one eight-hour shift basis. Only
Vojvodina and Serbia proper have sufficient livestock available for slaughter
uniformly throughout the year. Since the livestock supply in the other
republics and provinces is seasonal, 30 to 40% of the slaughter capacity is
required only during the 100 - 120 day peak periods. Several plants are re-
ported to have installed modern equipment for processing and packaging meats,
but installation is generally poor due to failure to integrate the equipment
into the total plant operations. Consequently, processed meat manufacture
is often more labor-intensive than warranted by the equipment present. Also,
plants are generally poorly designed for efficient throughput and expansion.
10,       Fruit and Vegetable Processing.  Most fruit and vegetable processing
plants are old and oriented towards highly seasonal crops or a very limited



ANNEX 2
Page 3
line of commodities.  Annual throughput averages only about 5,000 m tons and
the capacity of the plants is substantially larger. The item with the largest
volume produced by far is fruit juice, though less than half the country's
fruit and vegetable plants manufacture it. Normal procedure after extracting the
juice is to pasteurize and bulk store it at low temperature. Only when orders
are received is it repasteurized and canned, reflecting the limited canning
and thermal processing capacities of the canneries.
11.       Edible Oil Processing.  More than half the edible oil consumed in
Yugoslavia is of vegetable origin, and around half of the 170,000 m tons of
vegetable oil produced in 1971 (Table 5) was derived from imported soybeans
or crude soybean oil. A significantly larger sunflower seee crop was expected
in 1972, thus reducing the requirements for imports.
12.       Yugoslavia has a total of 23 fat and oil plants.  Fourteen of these
plants are equipped to crush almost 660,000 m tons of sunflower seed annually
on the basis of 24-hour production for 280 days; at a 42% extraction rate
this equates to 277,000 m tons of oil. In contrast to the extraction plants,
which in 1972 were expected to be operating at approximately 55% of capacity,
the refineries have been operating at between 80% and almost 100%. Refining
capacity has been increased in 1972 to 263,000 m tons annually, and based
on this figure the present production of crude oil will absorb about 65% of
the new refining capacity. Generally, both the extraction and refining plants
are rather old, some originally having been built over 40 years ago.  Only
five plants have hydrogenation facilities to manufacture solid shortening and
only five are equipped to manufacture margarine. Approximately 30% of the
edible vegetable oil manufactured is sold for industrial and institutional
purposes.
13.       Wine Processing.  The major portion of the raw primary wines is
prepared by both private farmers and social enterprises in small facilities
near the vineyard where grapes are pressed and fermented into wine. The
major wine processors outside Macedonia - Navip, Zupaki Tubin, Vino Zupa,
Slovenija Vino, Dalmacija Vino, Vinoprodukt and Istia Vino Export - procure
bulk raw wines throughout Yugoslavia for blending, stabilizing and bottling
under their brand for domestic and export sales. Between 1966 and 1970,
total wine cellar capacity in Yugoslavia increased from 320,000 tons to
400,000 tons. Most of the added capacity was large industrial type vessels
(up to 400,000 liters) which could be used for finalizing raw wines. In 1971
the total wine production in Yugoslavia was 555,000 tons. Although the nomi-
nal wine cellar capacity had increased to 450,000 tons in 1971, the effective
capacity was about 350,000 tons (about 30% of the total wine cellar capacity
is used for transfer stock and temporary in-process storage). Yugoslavia
exported raw wine to Austria, West Germany and U.K. for blending, stabilizing
and bottling.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
B. Macedonia
14.       The Republic of Macedonia contains approximately 80-100 food
processing plants producing around 300,000 tons of food plus about 200 million
1 of alcoholic and non-alcoholic beverages. In terms of volume, Macedonian
production of processed food products represents only 7.2% of food and 3.9%
of alcoholic and non-alcoholic beverages produced in Yugoslavia. The
M4acedonian food processing industry enjoyed an 8% annual growth rate from
1966 to 1970. In only one category, rice, does Macedonia dominate Yugoslavian
production and in only four sectors.-- ground paprika, bread and pastry, canned
vegetables and other vegetable preparations (e.g., pickles) -- does Macedonia
produce more than 10% of Yugoslavian output. Details on Macedonia's processed
food production are given in Table 6.
15.       Meat Processing.  In both Yugoslavia and Macedonia during the period
1968 thru 1971, meat production (carcass equivalent) has been erratic as
shown in the following table:
Yugoslavia                      Macedonia
Year           Beef     Pork      Lamb            Beef     Pork      Lamb
- - -'000 tons - -         -         - - '000 tons - - -
1968           269       373       55               7        5         9
1969            258      287       51               8        4         8                  @
1970            233      338       47               6        5         8
1971           242       384       50               5        6         9
Yugoslavia's industrial abattoirs produce only about 46% of the country's
processed meat needs. In Macedonia, industrial abattoirs with a slaughtering
capacity of about 10,000 tons produced about 30% of the Republic's meat con-
sumption; the private sector and imports contributed the remainder. The pro-
cessing plants are small and generally need more modern facilities. The
average annual production per industrial abattoir in Yugoslavia is 5,300 tons
while, in Macedonia, the average annual production is only 1,100 tons.  Three
of Macedonia's slaughterhouses are registered to export meat to all European
markets except the U.K.: Klanica so ladilnik in Skopje, PK Strumica in Strumica
(lamb only) and Gorni Polog in Gostivar (lamb only).  In addition to the in-
dustrial slaughterhouses, Macedonia also has about 30 very small abattoirs
which only slaughter for local markets. Macedonia lacks sanitary and efficient
meat processing facilities which are large enough to economically produce the
product mix necessary to supply domestic demand for fresh meat cuts, canned
and cured meat products as well as sausages. In order to export canned pork
and beef products to the U.S. and the U.K., canning facilities have to meet
rigid sanitary standards which the one existing plant that has a canning line
does not satisfy. With the increase in cattle and pig production projected
without the project, by 1980 Macedonia would have a deficit industrial                    0



ANNEX 2
Page 5
slaughtering capacity of 11,000 tons; with production as a result of the
project, this deficit would increase to 15,300 tons. Thus, the additional
processing capacity proposed as part of the project is urge.tly required
(Annex 9). In 1971, due to the lack of adequate slaughtering capacity,
Macedonia exported live animals valued at about US$ 4.8 million rather than
value added processed meat products.
16.       Fruit and Vegetable Processing.  Fruit and vegetable processing
facilities in Macedonia are generally small and inefficient. Despite the
presence of some modern mechanized and even automated equipment, total
systems are not employed and consequently, entire lines are controlled by
the least efficient element in the line.  Factories contain large numbers
of relatively low capacity equipment in order to be capable of processing
snall quantities of many different products. Batch operation is common,
even when continuous processing equipment is present. Labor is used on
lines in place of the most elemental equipment, in sequence with automated
machinery -- i.e., lines are not completely equipped. Floor supervision
appears to be lacking, and quality of operation and product output is mediocre.
Inputs are neither well-handled nor closely controlled and scheduling of
raw material appears to be a major factor contributing to the low efficiency.
Handling of the finished goods is generally manual leading to damaged packages
_        and consequent losses beyond line inefficiencies.
17.       Macedonian fruit and vegetable processing facilities might be
described as operating on a hand-to-mouth basis responding to immediate de-
mands of agricultural production or of a specific customer with only secondary
regard for processing operations or for non-food input or final product output
inventorying and logistics. Because of a lack of adequate bottling facilities,
fruit juices are extracted, concentrated, pasteurized and nacked in small bulk
containers for storage until time is available on the packaging line. The
concentrate is then reconstituted, packaged and repasteurized, thus downgrading
quality. Uniform procedures appear to be applied for preparing all natural
juices, thus leading to a loss of character identity in the final product.
Fruit for jams appears to be handled in much the same manner with the textural
contrasts characteristic of European jams being totally absent from the
Macedonian products. Despite the presence of some thermal processing equip-
ment, overprocessing is the rule to assure safety. Although not clean by
European standards, fruit and vegetable processing operations appear to be
satisfactory from a sanitary standpoint.
18.       Six vegetable processing factories are listed in Macedonia and three
also process fruits. Average annual production from these plants in 1970 was
2,500 tons of canned vegetables, 265 tons of "other processed vegetables", 230
tons of fruit semi-preparations without sugar, 390 tons of fruit preparations
without sugar, 220 tons of fruit preparations with sugar and 690 tons of
natural fruit juices for a total average, for those plants producing all
products, of 4,290 tons. On a countrywide basis, average annual factory out-
put in 1970 was just over 5,000 tons.
19.       The largest fruit and vegetable processing factory in Macedonia is
Konservaexport located in Skopje. Konservaexport has a maiximum plant capacity



ANNEX 2
Page 6
of 13,800 tons per year of processed fruit and vegetable products, but has
an actual output of only 6,000 to 8,000 tons. This output is divided among
some 50 products with fruit and vegetable juices accounting for about a third
of the volume. The factory packs canned vegetables, pickles, tomato concen-
trate, jams, etc. About 20% of the production is exported, mostly peppers
and pickled products to Germany and Austria. The Grocka-Tetova plant is re-
ported to have an annual capacity of about 12,000 tons but has an output of
only 8,000 tons, principally vegetable products such as peppers, ajvar and
beans. The Zora-Gevgelija operation is reported to have an annual capacity
of 5,500 tons and production is estimated at less than 4,000 tons, primarily
tomato concentrate, peppers and other indigenous Yugoslavian foods. Zik-
Strumica has a capacity of 7,000 tons a year with an output and oroduct mix
very similar to that of Zora-Gevgelija. Bitola-Pelagonia is reported to have
an annual capacity of about 4,500 tons with production being less than 3,000
tons, mainly tomato concentrate, pickles and fruit compotes. The Gradinar-Ohrid
factory has a capacity of 1,500 tons per year for pickles, green peas, peppers,
beans and tomato concentrate. This plant did not operate i. 1971, however, due
to a shortage of raw material. In addition to these operat_ as, Kumanova pro-
duces the Sinalco soft drink under license from a German franchiser and the
Zik-Povardarie-Negotina wine cellar produces Tika, a fortified natural grape
drink, in its wine-bottling operation. The estimated capacity of the six
fruit and vegetable processing plants in Macedonia is about 44,000 to 45,000
tons annually. With an actual output of 26,000 tons, the factories are operating
at about 60% of capacity.
20.       Edible Oil Processing.  A single edible vegetable oil processing
plant, Prehrana, is in operation in Macedonia in Titov Veles. The plant was
originally built over 40 years ago and much of the crushing equiprent dates
back to prior to World Wiar II. Solvent extraction equipment, however, is
relatively sew and the refinery has been reequipped in the last several years.
The extraction plant and refinery report an output capacity  v,f 22,400 tons
of sunflower seed oil per year based on 24-hour-a-day extra cLion, 280 days
a year. With 1970 output at 17,258 tons the plant operated at about 77%
capacity. 1/  The plant has a new automatic packaging line for one14lter re-
turnable glass bottles, which is the standard consumer package in -much of the
country. The bulk line, however, is still very much manual despite being
houised in the new packaging building.
21.       In addition to the edible oil operation, Prehrana devotes a re-
modeled portion of the plant to the production of margarine in 150-g consumer
packages. Margarine output capacity is approximately one ton per hour on
relatively modern equipment. The plant uses very little of its fat and oil
otutput as raw material for margarine manufacture because it has no hydrogena-
tion capability. Management reported importing about 350 tons of hydrogenated
oil from Norway in 1971 for the manufacture of margarine.
1/ A plant in Urosevac, only 120 km distant andJust across the border in
Serbia, operated at only 63% capacity in 1971, processing both sunflower
seed and crude soya oil.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
22.       Wine Processing.  In Macedonia, grape pressing and primary wine
fermentation facilities are generally small and lacking in sanitation and
technical expertise for producing quality wines. Most of the primary pro-
ducers belong to a cooperative enterprise (Makedonia Vino) which markets the
wines to processors in Yugoslavia and to importers in Austria, Germany, U.K.
and elsewhere. Four Macedonian raw wine producers also finalize and bottle
some of their better quality wines (about 2,000 tons yearly). Only one enter-
prise, Tikves, produces a significant quantity of finalized wine (about 12,000
tons) for domestic and export markets. Tikves' product mix has shifted from
50% bottled in 1969 to 70% in 1971, reflecting the increased demand for
finished and bottled wines. About 35% of Tikves' cellar capacity of 22,000
tons is required for processing finished wines, liquors and non-alcoholic
beverages. Due to the lack of an adequate sanitary and efficient central wine
cellar facility, the majority (65%) of the primary raw wines produced by the
private and social sector in Macedonia is marketed in bulk for processing out-
side the Republic.

YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
Financial and Institutional Aspects
A. Background
Central Banking Policy
1.        The Yugoslavian banking system, which was revised early in 1972,
now has separate national banks which act as central banks in each of the
six republics and two autonomous regions, together with over 70 business
banks. Each national bank is free to determine its own sectoral priorities
and allocate its share of the funds.
2.        The 1972 revision included several ad hoc measur_s to increase the
regulatory role of the national banks and improve the position of business
banks. Stricter penalties are now imposed on any business bank unable to
meet its financial obligations, including limitation of credit expansion for
a specified period. Business banks are affected by measures designed to
force creditors to bear the full burden of risk, and debtors to assume full
responsibility for their obligations. Separate long- and short-term balance
sheets for business banks are no longer required, leaving them free to manage
their funds subject only to liquidity criteria specified by the Federal
National Bank. To encourage further private savings, the recent ceiling on
interest rates has been abolished.
The Business Banks
3.        The business banks consist of investment, commercial and "mixed"
(combined investment and commercial) banks. They are controlled by the
economic and socio-political organizations that have invested in their fixed
capital. Any group of such organizations may establish a bank, subject to
federal minimum capital requirements. Each participant is represented in
the bank's Assembly in proportion to its contribution to the bank's initial
capital. The Assembly is the bank's highest administrative organ, and must
have a minimum of 25 members, each having at least one vote. The maximum
voting power of any one participant is 10% of the total. A special voting
quota in the Assembly is reserved for the bank's staff, and is fixed in
the bank's constitution. The Assembly appoints a Board of Management and a
Managing Director to supervise the bank's day-to-day business. Requests for
loans are decided by a Loan Committee, consisting of members of the bank's
staff, in accordance with regulations approved by the AssembtIy. Participants
in the bank's capital are entitled to a share in the profits. There are no
legal provisions for the withdrawal of a capital contribution, but its rights
are transferable. Further capital subscriptions with proportional voting
rights may be made at any time, and the rights are recalculated annually.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
4.        A bank's resources consist of:  its capital; sight, time and
savings deposits; funds acquired through bond issues and other securities;
government investment resources; and borrowings from the National Bank
and from abroad. Time deposits are 'usually made under a contract for a
minimum of three months, while some give the depositor voting rights in the
Assembly. Sight deposits come from economic and socio-political organizations,
while savings deposits come mainly from the public.  Savings deposits and
interest on them are exempt from taxes, and guaranteed by the Federal Govern-
ment. Bond issues, and trade in them, are regulated at federal level. State
investments are generally channeled through the investment sections of the
banks, with the government (federal or republic) retaining the right to
allocate them for specific sectors or individual projects.  Commercial banks
(and some economic organizations) are now permitted to borrow direct from
abroad, subject to regulations, and twenty operate foreign exchange departments.
Short-term rediscounting through the national banks, although subject to a
number of conditions and limitations, is available to all business banks.
5.        The share of state-generated savings in total savings has declined
from an average of 36% for 1960-64 to 21% in 1966 and 15% in 1970. The share
of the social sector increased from about 52% in 1960-64 to about 61% in 1968,
but declined to about 55% in 1970, due almost entirely to pressure on self-
managed enterprises to distribute more of their surplus in incomes. The
share of private sector savings increased from about 9% ir; 1960-64 to about
27% in 1966-70. Continuing efforts to mobilize private savings are necessary
to maintain the overall savings rate, and the recent abolition of the interest
ceiling on them has increased their attraction.
Resource Allocation
6.        The state's former role in mobilizing and allocating resources has
largely been taken over by the banks. During 1960-64, state financing
accounted for about 56% of total investment resources in the social sector,
but it declined to 15% in 1970. In the same period the share of investment
banks increased from 10% to 52%.
7.        The main channel for transferring resources to the underdeveloped
regions is the Federal Fund for the Accelerated Development of Less Developed
Republics and Regions (FFAD). The responsibility for financing it has re-
centl. been transferred from the Federal Government to the republics, which
nov have to find their own share of the total.  Each republic's quota is met
by compulsory loans from economic enterprises within it. These are passed
on to the FFAD as long-term loans over 15 years at 4% annual interest, and
relent to designated banks in the underdeveloped regions at 4.5%, the ad-
ditional 0.5% being a service charge levied by the FFAD. Previously, alloc-
ations from the Fund were grants to designated banks for on-lending to social
enterprises. The assets arising out of past grants from the Fund have been
transferred to the republics benefitting from them.



ANNEX 3
*                                                                            Page 3
8.        A major objective of IBRD's current lending program in Yugoslavia
is acceleration of development in the less developed regions including
Macedonia, particularly through improved resource allocation. By increasing
interest rates to subborrowers (para 45), the proposed project would con-
tribute to this objective.
Banking Statistics
9.        Table 1 gives a summary of the consolidated balance sheets of all
banks in Yugoslavia at the end of the three years 1966, 1968 and 1970. Total
assets increased by over 40%, from ND 148 billion in 1966 to ND 212 billion
in 1970. Short-term and investment loans outstanding increased by over 60%,
from ND 106 billion in 1966 to ND 173 billion in 1970. The proportion of
investment loans in this total declined from 74% in 1966 to 61% in 1970. Over
the same period, savings and time deposits more than doubled.
10.       Voting rights held by the various types of organizations in the
banks' Assemblies at the end of 1970 were as follows:
Organization        Yugoslavia         Macedonia        Slovenia
------p-____------ per cent ---------- --
Economic                64.8               48.5             79.6
Socio-political         18.7              28.4              11.3
Others                  16.5               23.1              9.1
Total        100.0             100.0            100.0
In Macedonia, the share held by economic organizations is the lowest in the
country, and by socio-political organizations the highest. However, the
share held by economic organizations is increasing each year. The situation is
similar in other less developed regions, and contrasts with that in Slovenia,
the most developed republic.
11.       The total number of employees in business banks was almost 20,000
at the end of 1970, of which 1,200 (or about 6%) were in Macedonian banks.
The average monthly salary received per bank employee in 1970 was about ND
1,480 for Yugoslavian banks as a whole, and about ND 1,440 for Macedonian
banks.
The Accounting System
12.       Yugoslav financial principles for economic enterprises differ from
those generally applied in non-socialist countries in the concept of ownership,
priority of obligations, and disposition of net income. They do, however,
recognize the need to generate from revenue sufficient cash to cover all
operating expenses (including depreciation), to provide funds for debt service
and additional investments in enterprises and in social facilities for the
*           workers.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
13.       The current federal law on the Accounting of Working Organizations
became effective on January 1, 1969. While it does little more than oblige
every enterprise to keep an accurate set of business records using double-
entry bookkeeping methods, it severely limits the former legal authority
of both regional and central governments to call for accounts and reports.
Its primary purpose is to provide information to the management of each
enterprise to enable it to make decisions and exercise intetnal controls,
but it also provides limited information to the state. The fiscal year for
all enterprises coincides with the calendar year, but inventories do not
necessarily have to be checked on December 31, although an annual check is
mandatory.
14.       All accounts have to be kept in a form laid down by law.  The basic
outline contains about 170 control accounts, all coded, and these have to be
followed by all enterprises. The outline can only be changed by amending
the law. Nobody outside the enterprise has any legal authority to call for
any additional information, except the court during an official investigation.
Individual enterprises may subdivide any of the 3-figure coded accounts to
suit their own purpose, but must make out all reports in the official form
only.
15.       Accounts are not audited in the strict sense of the term, but are
verified once a year by the Social Accounting Service (SAS), and examined by
it in greater detail at irregular intervals of between one and three years.
The main purpose of the verification is to examine whether the financial
transactions comply with Yugoslav law.  Discussions are currently underway
among IBRD, UNDP and the Federal Government whereby 20 officers of the SAS
would be trained in international auditing principles. When qualified, these
officers would be used in the first instance to assist in auditing the accounts
of organizations and projects for which IBRD has provided finance.
Interest Rates
16.       As a result of the lifting of the ceiling on interest rates at
the beginning of 1972, mainly as an inducement to private savings, average
annual rates at business banks on deposits, which were formerly 6% to 8%,
have now increased to 7% on sight, 9% on 1 to 2 years, and 10% on more than
2 years. The rate at which the regional central banks will at present redis-
count short-term paper from the business banks averages about 6%. The rates
at ,1Ji   business banks on-lend to borrowers depend mainly on their source of
funas, but are generally 1 to 1-1/2% above the rates they pay on borrowed
funds. Lending rates vary considerably, but average from around 5% for long-
term development projects using state funds, up to about 11% for short-term
commercial loans. Rates for agriculture are discussed below.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
B. Finance for Agriculture
Background
17.       The various types of agricultural enterprises are described else-
where in this report (Annex 1). Agro-industrial enterprises (combinats
and cooperatives) obtain their finance through the business banks. Through
various forms of cooperation with combinats or cooperatives, private farmers
obtain finance, goods and services such as seeds and fertilizer, sales and
marketing.
18.       The most widespread cooperation between private farmers and the
social sector is in the supply of services, such as land preparation, harvest-
ing, threshing, transport, and sometimes seasonal inputs, by agro-combinats
and cooperatives. Most services are given as a seasonal credit, with payment
in goods or cash at harvest. Thus, the social enterprises frequently serve
as processors or marketers of private farm products. The combinats usually
lay down technical standards. Under various joint production arrangements,
some combinats occasionally supply limited long-term credit to private farmers
for on-farm development.
Sector Finance
19.       Agriculture's share of total investment in Yugoslavia for the three
years 1967 through 1969 was as follows:
1967        1968          1969
---------- percent ----------
Economic
Agriculture                8.6          7.4           8.0
All other sectors         66.0         67.0          66.0
Subtotal    74.6         74.4          74.0
Socio-political                25.4         25.6          26.0
Total      100.0        100.0         100.0
20.       Over the three years, agricultural investment averaged 8% of total
investment or 11% of all economic investment. Most agricultural loans were
for planting or replanting of tree crops, land purchase and improvement, and
modernization and new capacity for agro-industries. They ranged from 30% to
60% of total project cost, depending on the nature of the project and the
policies of the lending bank. Loans for land purchase were usually repayable
in up to 20 years, but for most other purposes between 5 and 15 years, de-
pending on the financial return of the project.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
21.       Over the past five years, about three-fourths of all agricultural
investment has been in the social, and only one-fourth in the private sector.
In the social sector, about two-fifths has been for land, planting, replanting
and factory construction, and about one-third for equipment and its installa-
tion. About one-seventh of private sector investment has been for equipment
over this period.
22.       By far the largest source of investment finance for the agricultural
sector is the Yugoslav Agricultural Bank, Belgrade. Over the period 1966-71,
62% of its total investment credits disbursed were in the agricultural sector:
39% crops and livestock, 21% agro-industries, and 2% forestry. This was about
one-third of all agricultural investment during the period. At the end of
1971, its total resources were about ND 15.7 billion (of which fixed capital
was about 27%), making it the fifth largest bank in Yugoslavia.
Interest Rates
23.       The current interest rate policies of business banks have been
discussed above (para 16). Prior to the beginning of 1972, rates for agricul-
tural loans were subsidized by the Federal Government. For short-term loans,
the borrower paid 3%, the Government 5%, and the lending bank thus received
8%. On longer term loans, the Government gave a 3% subsidy, provided the bank
charged a maximum of 6% to the borrower, the bank thus receiving 9% maximum.
24.       From January 1, 1972, subsidized interest rates became the respon-
sibility of the separate republics and regions, and policy varies in each.
In Macedonia, the Government has made a clear distinction between funds on-
lent from bank's own sources and those from other sources. Agricultural
investment loans made from Government funds by business banks to borrowers
in the social sector are at the following rates:
Annual %
Irrigation               3
On-farm mechanization   5
All other purposes        5.5
For irrigation loans, the Government source is usually the Macedonia Invest-
ment F nd srhich lends to banks at 2%. For the other loans the source is
the .FAD (para 7), which lends to banks at 4.5%. Investment loans made to
borrowers in the social sector by business banks from their own resources
are at the same rates as above, but the Government provides a subsidy enabl-
ing the banks to earn 8%. For investment loans to the private sector, FFAD
funds are lent to agro-combinats and cooperatives at 5.5% and on-lent to
private farmers at 6.0%-6.5%. Investment loans to the private sector made
by business banks from their own resources are channelled through agro-combi-
nats and cooperatives at 6.0% and are on-lent at 6.5% to 7.0%. These loans
are often eligible for Government subsidies of up to 6.0%, reflecting a greater
risk in lending to the private sector.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
25.       The major source of funds for short-term loans is the business
banks' own resources, the current cost of which is, on average, about 8%.
These funds are lent by banks to the social sector at 5.0%-5.5% and where
they are on-lent to private farmers, the social sector usually adds a spread
of 0.5%.
C.  The Macedonian Banks
Background
26.       Prior to their merger into a single legal and operational entity in
January 1973, the three banks listed below were registered in Macedonia:
(a) Stopanska Bank, Skopje (SBS);
(b) Commercial and Investment Bank, Skopje (CIBS); and
(c) Commercial and Investment Bank, Bitola (CIBB).
They are more fully described below. Their share of the total assets and
liabilities of all Yugoslavian banks is slightly under 9%. Between them they
conducted, and the surviving Stopanska Bank is expected to continue to do,
about 95% of all the banking business in Macedonia, the remainder being hand-
led by subsidiaries of other Yugoslav banks.
Stopanska Bank, Skopje
27.       In this Annex, the pre-merger SBS is examined in greater detail than
the other two banks, because it contains the organization which will deal with
project loans under the merged bank and is the dominant partner in the merger.
SBS' main activities over the past five years have been as an investment bank,
although more recently it has enlarged its commercial banking section, parti-
cularly to obtain a larger share of private savings, in view of their increas-
ing importance as a source of funds (para 5). Most of its investment has been
in industry, including agricultural processing. It also operates a foreign
exchange section. It had no branches outside Skopje.
28.       SBS' constitution followed that for business banks set out in para-
graph 3 above. Its Assembly had increased from 54 members at the end of 1967
to 65 at the end of 1971, made up as follows:
0



ANNEX 3
Page 8
Economic enterprises
Industry and mining            27
Agriculture                      9
Trading and catering             8
Communications                   4
Civil engineering                3
Subtotal             51
Banks                                 8
Socio-political organizations         2
Other (including SBS staff)           4
Total                65
On the same date the Executive Board consisted of 19 members, including the
Director General of SBS and a staff representative. Its Loans Board comprised
8 senior staff members, and the Director General as Chairman. In 1971 the
Loans Board held 59 meetings covering 571 loans and related affairs. There
were 23 members on the SBS Workers' Council.
29.       The bank's business was conducted through 11 departments, three
independent groups, an internal inspectorate and a secretariat. These
included an Agriculture and Forestry Loans Department, responsible for all
loans to the agricultural sector. The independent groups were concerned with
legal affairs, engineering and technical advice, and statistics. SBS staff
position at the end of each year, by educational standard, has been as follows:
1967    1968      1969     1970      1971
University             49        56       62        61       66
College                13        15       17        18       18
Secondary              82       83        79        83       98
Other                  47        47       45        48       50
Total            191      201       203      210       232
The most noticeable increase has been among university graduates, reflecting
lie desire of SBS management to increase its own quality and effectiveness.
The increase in staff at secondary level in 1971 reflects the enlargement of
SBS commercial banking activities.
30.       The Agriculture and Forestry Loans Department is divided into four
main sections and a secretariat, as set out in the attached organigram (chart),
and is headed by a Director and an Assistant Director. The main sections are:
livestock; production (mainly cereals, vegetables and fruits); processing
(mainly food and tobacco); and irrigation. There were 20 technical staff in
these sections as of December 1972; five additional staff joined the depart-
ment as a result of the merger.  Proposals for increasing the staff to cover
project requirements are discussed in the main report.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
31.       Financial data on SBS are given in Tables 2 throuigh 5.  The figures
show that of its total assets of ND 11.4 billion at the end of 1971, about
80% was invested in longer term loans (over 2 years) for industry, mining,
agriculture and communications. They also show the decline in time and sight
deposits over the past three years. However, sight deposits at June 30, 1972,
show an improvement to ND 0.50 billion, reflecting the increased effort in 1972
to attract them. The share of agriculture (including forestry and agro-
industries) in SBS' total lending program has averaged 15% over the past three
years, as follows:
1967     1968       1969      1970      1971
------ % of total loans outstanding ------
Agriculture
Investment           6.1      8.3        12.4       10.8     16.2
Short-term           na       na          2.9        1.8      1.1
na       na         15.3       12.6     17.3
Most of the investment funds for agriculture over this period have come from
the FFAD.
32.       SBS' current interest rates were fixed in January 1971, at the start
of the current five-year development plan. They are given in Table 5, which
shows that rates for agriculture are currently less than for industry and for
commerce. Over the past three years the spread on interest rates (being the
difference between interest received and interest paid out) to recover the
cost of administration (including taxes, staff emoluments and prudent reserve
allocations) has averaged about 0.8% to almost 1.0% in 1971 (Table 3). Because
of higher staff salaries and administrative costs, it is estimated to be al-
most 1.25% during the last quarter of 1972.
33.       SBS makes loans for agricultural investment only to the agro-
combinats and cooperatives within Niacedonia. Loans for irrigation are rarely
for more than 10 years, and for machinery and most other purposes are for a
maximum of five years, with grace periods where given geared to the cash flow
of the investment. In accordance with the almost universal Yugoslav banking
practice, SBS does not ask for securities (in the accepted sense of the word)
for loans made, and neither the Federal nor Republican Governments provide
guarantees. In effect, SBS relies on its own evaluation of each project,
since this includes an estimate of repayment ability. These evaluations are
thorough, and in the agricultural sector they have proved satisfactory.
The 1972 revision of the business banks (para 2) has reinforced banks' ability
to obtain redress from defaulting creditors, and this has strengthened SBS'
position. SBS investment loans overdue by more than 24 months at the end
of the last five years have averaged about 0.8% of total investment loans
outstanding each year, which is very satisfactory.
0



ANNEX 3
Page 10
Commercial and Investment Bank, Skopje
34.       CIBS was formed in 1956 with a single office in Skopje.  After
mergers with a number of smaller banks, mostly in 1968, its operations before
the merger covered most of Macedonia through 12 branches and 17 sub-branches.
It was not very active in the Bitola area, which was more completely covered
by CIBB (below, para 38). It did far more short term and commercial banking
business than SBS, and in investment was particularly active in the tourism
and building sectors. It also operated a foreign exchange division.
35.       CIBS' constitution followed that for all business (para 3).  At the
end of 1971 its Assembly consisted of about 50 members, and the Executive
Board 23 members.  Total staff was about 850.  The Managing Director was
assisted by two deputies and one assistant, together with 10 directors of
separate departments. Summarized balance sheets, and income and expenditure
statements are given in Tables 6 and 7. With total assets of about ND 11.3
billion at the end of 1971, CIBS was about the same size as SBS.
36.       A brief summary of CIBS operations for 1969 and 1970 is given in the
following table:

37.       The following table shows that CIBS paid more ir.terest on its

1969      1970       1971

This is mainly because it relied to a much greater extent on the relatively
expensive short-term deposits as a source of funds than does SBS. Over the
three years SBS' average spread was about 0.8% compared with CIBS 1.5%. This
was due to the more commercial nature of CIBS business, which required more
staff than SBS (850 compared to 230 at the end of 1971), and thus a greater
spread to meet costs.
Commercial and Investment Bank, Bitola
38.       CIBB was a much smaller bank than SBS and CIBS, with total assets
of about ND 1.6 billion at the end of 1971, or only about one-seventh of those
of the other two. In addition to the head office in Bitola, it had six branches
in that area. Total staff was about 170. Around one-half of its funds came
from deposits, mostly sight, and much of its business was commercial short-
term. At the end of 1971, short-term advances outstanding were slightly over
one-third of its total assets. Its interest rate policy was similar to that
of CIBS.
D. The Merger of Macedonian Banks
Background
39.       Since 1965 the trend throughout Yugoslavia for business banks to
merge has increased. CIBS merged with a number of other banks in 1968. The
merger of SBS, CIBS and CIBB carried the trend to its logical conclusion in
Macedonia, since they were the only three banks registered in the Republic,
although there is one other bank operating in it through local branches.



ANNEX 3
Page 12
This is the Bank of Yugoslavia, registered in Serbia (Belgrade), but it does
only about 5% of all banking business in the Republic. The Yugoslav Industrial
Bank and the Ytgoslav Agricultural Bank also operate in the Republic, but
mainly through association with SBS, and they have no local branches.
The Merger
40.       The three banks are complementary, and their merger will not create
any new monopoly. SBS brings to the merger its development finance expertise,
particularly in industry and agriculture, while CIBS brings in its investment
experience in tourism and building, plus its commercial banking experience in
all areas except where CIBB operates. CIBB brings in its commercial and in-
vestment bankihg experience in its operating area.
41.       The three banks merged completely to form one new legal entity
in January 19t3. Table 8 shows what the combined balance sheet would have
been had the merger taken place on January 1, 1972. The combined assets
would then have been about ND 24 billion, making it the fourth largest bank
(in terms of assets) in Yugoslavia. According to an earlier SBS' estimate,
the relative amounts of share capital brought to the merger on January 1,
1973, were to be: SBS 49%, CIBS 44%, and CIBB 7%, confirming SBS' dominance
in the merger.
42.       Apart from the addition of five technical staff from CIBS and CIBB,
the Agriculture and Forestry Loans Department would rem2ain as formerly
under SBS. Most of the organizational changes are likely to be in those
departments more closely concerned with commercial banking.
Project Interest Rates
43.       SBS and CIBS participated almost equally in the merger.  Their
spreads, based on the past four years' operations, have been as follows (see
also para 37):
1969      1970       1971      1972/a
percent 
SBS       0.80       0.58      0.96       1.20
CTB8      1.39       1.59      1.46       1.65
Average   1.10       1.09       1.21      1.43
/a   Estimated.
44.       Assuming a contribution by IBRD of about 60% to all subloans (at
7,25%), a 30% contribution by FFAD at 4.5%, and 10% from the merged banks'
own resources, which presently cost about 8.0%, the blend cost of funds to
the merged banks would be just over 6.5% (Table 9).



ANNEX 3
Page 13
45.       The mission recommends that the interest rate payable by sub-
borrowers be 8.25% per annum. In addition to enabling elimination of Govern-
ment interest subsidies on SBS's own resources contribution, it would allow
for a possible increase in the cost of SBS's own funds, and at the same time
ensure a spread of between 1.6% and 1.7% to cover administrative, appraisal
and other costs under the project (Table 9). A spread of at least 1.5% is
considered necessary to provide sufficient incentive to the on-lenders of
project funds to carry out the aims of the project. An on-lending rate of
8.25% also provides for an increase of more than 50% in the current lending
rates, and as such would be a major step towards a more rational credit
policy for agriculture. Since financial rates of return on all subprojects
are well over 12% (Annex 10), a rate of 8.25% should not prove an undue
financial burden on subborrowers and should encourage a more efficient use
of scarce resources.
