YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
Terms of Reference for Technical Assistance Studies
A. Objectives of the Studies
1.        The proposed project provides US$0.6 million to help finance three
comprehensive studies, as detailed below. The studies will define future
investment programs for the development of Macedonia's agriculture, particu-
larly the private sector.
2.        The three studies are:
a.   Irrigation Study,  Determination of what is needed to finish
the partially completed irrigation projects in Prespa, Strumitsa,
Tikves and Bregalnitsa, development an investment plan for access
roads for the system and determination the benefits these invest-
ments would provide for Macedonia;
b.   Marketing Study.  Determination of the potential long-term
demand for food products (domestic and export) and recommen-
dation whether Macedonia should establish additional food pro-
cessing industries such as fruit and vegetable and oilseeds; and
C.   Private Farm Credit Study.  Determination of the benefits
generated from the first phase private farmers' credit program
financed under the project and preparation of a feasibility
study for a second phase credit program for private farm de-
velopment within Macedonia.
3.        The Stopanska Bank (SBS) will be responsible for implementing the
studies which would be carried out under contracts awarded as a result of
competitive bidding according to Bank guidelines.  SBS will designate a co-
ordinator to supervise and guide the studies and to assure the distribution
of data and information among the contractors.
4.        The estimated costs and manpower requirements of the studies are
as follows:
Cost            Manpower
(US$ thousands)    (man-months)
Irrigation Study                         340                 48
Marketing Study                          120L                18
Private Farm Credit Study               (150)                72
Total                    610                138



ANNEX 5
Page 2
B.  Irrigation Feasibility Study                                      g
1.        Scope of Work.  A consultant firm shall be contracted by MMB to
assist the Government of Macedonia with an irrigation feasibility study to
update and complete existing (preliminary) studies for the partially com-
pleted Prespa, Tikves, Strumitsa and Bregalnista irrigation systems, to
develop the investment program for improving access roads within private
sector production areas and to determine benefits of such investment pro-
grams to the private sector.
2.        The study will include:
a.   A general review of the status of other irrigation projects
under construction, including:
i.   location,
ii.   number of hectares involved,
iii.   number of farmers involved,
iv.   distribution of hectares between social and private sectors,
v.   source of water supply,
vi.   delivery capacity of each system,
vii.   description of system,
viii.   estimated project costs,
ix.   date of commencement and description of work completed,
x.   estimated completion dates,
xi.   projected water costs, and
xii.   existing and proposed cropping patterns and yields.
b.  A review of the general climatic cogditions prevailing in
Macedonia, including:
i.   average precipitation by month for past 10 years in mm,
ii.   average monthly temperature in 'C,
iii.   maximum and minimum recorded temperatures per month in °C,
iv.   average sunshine per month in hours, and
v.   average evapotranspiration (KWl) in no.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
c.   Analysis of water availability for irrigation in Macedonia,
including:
i.   a detailed review of water sources and availability,
ii.   flow data for rivers by month,
iii.   estimated storage capacities for reservoirs and dams,
iv.   a review of water quality,
v.   projected water usage,
vi.   groundwater usage, and
vii.   drainage systems.
d.   Updating the existing four feasibility studies for the Tikves,
Prespa, Strumitsa and Bregalnista irrigation projects, including:
i.   a detailed review and analysis of the partially
completed water distribution systems (canals, tunnels,
dams and field networks),
ii.   definition of the organization of the water management
enterprises responsible for operation of the system
in each region,
w.    iii.   a detailed evaluation of the delivery capacities and
potential for expanding the present systems,
iv.   a review of the present cropping patterns in all
four regions, with costs and revenues of production,
v.   the development of cropping patterns with and without
the project, for both the private and social sectors,
based upon the most economic use of irrigatcd land
resources and the potential markets for production,
including costs of production and revenues,
vi.   preparation of the water requirements for the system
from the proposed cropping patterns,
vii.   preparation of detailed engineering studies, where
necessary, to provide sufficient capacity to meet
demand. Particular emphasis would be placed on water
supply during July and August when little or no rainfall
is received,



ANNEX 5
Page 4
viii.   updating the cost estimates for construction
including those necessary to modify partially
completed or undersized storage and/or delivery
systems,
ix.   preparation of estimated operating costs for each
system and the projected water charges and
construction timetable,
X.   examination of potential markets for project crops,
xi.   determination of the financial feasibility of the
proposed investments, and
xii.   determination of the economic cost-benefits to
Macedonia resulting from the proposed project.
e.   Updating the proposed access road project, including:
i.   a review of class IV access roads in Macedonia,
ii.   establishment with G6vernment of priorities for
improving access roads taking into consideration
long-range agricultural development plans,
iii.   the development of an investment program for access
road improvement,
iv.   preparation of a detailed engineering design and
specifications for proposed project,
v.   preparation of cost estimates,
vi.   proposal for organization of construction,
vii.   preparation of time schedules,
viii.   determination of the expected traffic patterns for
each proposed road, and
ix.   the development of cost-benefit justification for the
investments.
3.        The consultants shall prepare a list of goods ane services with
unit prices for all major items of the proposed projects, identifying the
foreign and local currency components for each item and showing import
duties as a separate entry.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
Economic Evaluation
4.        The consultant shall carry out a financial and economic evaluation
of the proposed project including cost estimates and detailed work programs.
a.   Included will be financial forecasts and analyses of costs
of prospective input and supporting services and of revenues.
The consultants will prepare operating revenue and expense
statements, cash flow statements and investment forecasts and
calculate rate of return on net fixed assets.
b.   The consultants shall estimate and analyze the economic costs
and benefits of the proposed projects. The benefits from
the proposed projects would include estimates of savings to
the private farmer in the reduction of shipping losses, lack
of product damage and spoilage, improved prices for output,
expanded markets, cheaper input prices and transpsrtation
charges, improved crop yields, higher value crops, etc.
Costs will include construction and equipment acquisition,
installation and maintenance. The final economic evaluation
will include determination of the economic rate of return on
investment by discounting future benefits and costs to equal
present values.
5.        The consultants shall cooperate fully with SBS and Government,
which will provide the necessary data, facilities and services outlined in
paras 6 and 7. The consultants shall be solely responsible for the analyses
and interpretation of data received and for the findings, conclusions and
recommendations contained in the reports. SBS will facilitate the liaison
with the consultants undertaking the market study to ensure that they have
access to all information necessary for completing their study.
Support Services
6.        SBS and the Government of Macedonia shall furnish the consultants
all relevant statistical data, feasibility reports for the Tikves, Prespa,
Strumitsa and Bregalnista irrigation projects, access road data, maps and
other information available. The consultants shall not publish or disclose
any of the information so obtained without written consent of SBS.
7.        SBS and Government's assignment of clerical assistance, technicians,
office accommodations, vehicles and local equipment will be mutually agreed
during contract negotiations.
Time Schedule and Reports
8.        The study will require nine months for compiling and analyzing data
and three months for preparation of the final report.



ANNEX 5
Page 6
9.        The consultants shall submit the following reports to SBS:
a.   Progress reports - bimonthly (ten copies in English).
b.   A draft final report - within 60 days after completion of
studies (20 copies in English).
C.   A final report - within 30 days after receipt of SBS's
comments on draft final report (50 copies in English).
d.   In addition to the above, the consultants shall furnish
SBS with such information as it may reasonably request
to follow progress of the study. All reports and comments
will become the exclusive property of the Government of
Macedonia.
10.       The proposed study is estimated to cost about US$340,000; it will
require about 48 man-months, and will require the following expertise:
a.   Irrigation Expert - Project leader responsible for organiza-
tion of the study, preparation of progress and final reports,
liaison with marketing study project leader, etc. This person
should have long experience in irrigation systems development.
b.   irrigation and Civil Engineer(s)
C.   Agriculturalist
d.   Agricultural Economist
e.   Financial Analyst
f.   Back-up staff in home office.
C. Marketing Study
1.        Scope of Work.  A consultant firm shall be contracted by SBS to
assist the Government of Macedonia with a marketing study to assess the future
demand and supply of principal agricultural products in the Republic as well
as in the other parts of Yugoslavia.  In addition, the study will determine
the demand and supply of these commodities in other major producing and con-
suming countries including both Western and Eastern Europe, and assess whether
Macedonia should establish additional food processing industries to supply
future domestic and export demand. The findings will assist Government in
formulating long-range development programs for agriculture both in the pri-
vate and sbcial sectors.
0



ANNEX 5
Page 7
2.        The consultants shall be responsible for collecting and analyzing
information and data necessary to satisfy the objectives of the study, in-
cluding the following:
a.   A broad review of Government policies and plans (both the
Republic and the Federal Government) for production in the
agricultural sector, including:
i.   production targets, controls and quotas,
ii.   subsidies,
iii.   prices, price policies and controls,
iv.   export targets and controls,
v.   import and export duties,
vi.   marketing institutions and practices,
vii.   transportation,
viii.   quality controls (standards),
ix.   inspection, and
x.   storage availability and costs.
b.   An examination of the supply potential of important agricultural
commodities in Macedonia and Yugoslavia, broken down between the
private and social sectors, including:
i.   fruit,
ii.   vegetables,
iii.   tobacco,
iv.   edible oil seeds,
v.   cereals,
vi.   livestock,
vii.   dairy products, and
viii.   poultry products.
c.   An examination of present and potential demand for each of the
commodities, broken down by Republics as well as by rural
and urban areas.  Detailed assessment of future demand will be
undertaken for the Republic of Macedonia only and Yugoslavia.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
d.   An assessment of the future external supply of the above
commodities, particularly from Rumania, Hungary and Bulgaria,
which have recently increased their production of a number
of fruits and vegetables in cooperation with Western fruit
and vegetable processing companies for marketing in the
EEC and other European countries.
e.   An assessment of demand for these commodities in the EEC
and other European and non-European countries.
f.   An assessment of both domestic and international prices.
3.        The consultants shall, based upon the analysis of the data in para
2, make recommendations concerning the channels and form of marketing, includ-
ing, for example, recommendations on how much should be sold in fresh form,
and how much should be sold after processing.
4.        The consultants shall review the processing capacity and potential
of food processing facilities in Yugoslavia to develop recommendationsc for
future investments in food plants3 in Macedonia. Included would be eccinomic
benefits and justifications for such programs.
5.        The consultants shall cooperate with, and provide projections and
other marketing information to, the consultants preparing the irrigation and
private farmer credit studies.
6.        The consultants shall make recommendations for institutional services             0
for the collection and dissemination of marketing information on a continuing
basis to private farmers, combinats, business enterprises and Government.
Support Services
7.        SBS and the Government of Macedonia shall furnish the consultants
all relevant statistical data, maps, long-range development plans and other
information available. The consultants shall not publish or disclose any of
the information so obtained without written consent of SBS.
8.        SBS and Government's assignment of clerical assistance, technicians,
office accommodations, vehicles and local equipment will be mutually agreed
upon durinn  contract negotiations.
Time Schedule and Reports
9.        The study will require six months for compiling and analyzing data
and three months for preparation of the final report.
10.       The consultants shall submit the following reports to SBS:
6



ANNEX 5
Page 9
a.   Progress reports - bimonthly (ten copies in English).
b.   A draft final report - within 60 days after completion of study
(20 copies in English).
c.   A final report - within 30 days after receipt of SBS's comments
on draft final report (50 copies in English).
d.   In addition to the above, the consultants shall furnish SBS
with such information as it may reasonably request to follow
progress of the study.  All reports and documents will become
the exclusive property of the Government of Macedonia.
11.       The proposed study is estimated to cost about US$120,000 and would
require about 18 man-months, consisting of the following expertise:
a.   Agricultural Marketing Specialist - project leader,
responsible for organization of study, liaison with
;MB and other irrigation and private farmer credit
studies consultants. This person should have wide
experience in the marketing of agricultural commodi-
ties and processed foods, in developing supply and
demand projections and in Government planning
_                   operations.
b.   Agricultural Economist
c.   Food Processing Engineer
d.   Marketing Experts.
D. Private Farmer Study
General Terms of Reference
1.        W'ithin six months of loan signing, SBS shall contract a consultant
firm, a university, or other entity, to:
a.   design and operate a system for monitoring and evaluating
the results of subproject investments in the private sector;
b.   assist Government with a study of priorities for development
of the private farm sector over the next ten years. The
study would concentrate particularly on the necessary infra-
structure, including extension and training services and
.



ANNEX 5
Page 10
long- and short-term credit requirements, to support desired
patterns of production and levels of farmer income; and
c.   prepare a private farm investment program.
Specific Terms of Reference
Monitoring and Evaluation of Project
2.        The Consultant shall design a farm record keeping system for use
by private farmer subborrowers with assistance from the combinats. Such
records would include:
a.   value (and number) of assets and liabilities before development;
b.   details of operations - physical and financial - before
development;
c.   number, value and nature of investments planned and their
timing;
d.   number, value and nature of investments actually made and their
timing;
e.   sources and terms of investment funds for inputs -made in
(d) above;
f.   amount and timing of loan amortization payments, projected and
actual;
g.   the annual short-term credit requirements (in both cash and
kind) before and after development;
h.   annual quantity and value of total production, sales and
on-farm consumption, before and after development;
i.   annual expenses and production costs, before atid after
development, with details of important items; an"
j.   annual changes in the value (and number) of physical assets.
3.   The Consultant shall:
a.  |develop a method for analyzing the farm records data.  If
the analysis involves the use of computer facilities and
the preparation of a computer program, the Consultant shall
take into account the possibility for future expansion of
the program to include the monitoring and evaluation of
subprojects other than those in the private farm sector;



ANNEX 5
Page 11
b.   assist SBS in defining the responsibilities of SBS,
combinat and farmers' cooperative subloan appraisal and
supervisory staff with respect to the implementation of
the record-keeping system; and
c.   conduct such training sessions as may be necessary.
4.        The Consultant shall review the immediate market alternatives
available to private farmers for their output; product pri:zs during and
after harvest; storage availability, use and costs; and any 3easonal or
annual product surpluses or shortages.
5.        The Consultant shall review Government policies affecting prices,
and crop and livestock production from project farms.
6.        The Consultant shall prepare annual progress reports, including 20
copies in English, for five years summarizing the information presented in
paras 2, 4 and 5. In addition, these reports shall include analyses of the
following:
a.   the costs of the lending program;
b.   comparison of interest rates available to private farmers
with those of other sectors and regions;
c.   the effect of agricultural subsidies and inflation on the
size and purpose of loans;
d.   the extent of overdue and delinquent loans and the reasons
therefore;
e.   the expected financial return on typical subproject
investments; and
f.   the effect of weather conditions on seasonal production.
7.        Within the five years contracted for the preparation of the annual
progress reports (para 6), SBS, Government and IBRD may mutually agree that
MMB has developed sufficient expertise to prepare such reports. If this is
the case, the Consultant would be released from the relevant article or
articles of his contract and the responsibility for preparing the reports
would be transferred to SBS.
8.        The Consultant shall complete the operations defined in paras 2
and 3 within six months of appointment.



ANNEX 5
Page 12
Development of Private Farm Sector
9.        The Consultant shall review the economic plans and policies for the
agricultural sector of both the Republic of Macedonia and the Federal Govern-
ment, including:
a.   the functions of existing agencies - agricultural banks,
commercial banks, combinats and cooperatives - with
particular regard to agricultural credit; and
b.   the present agricultural credit policies of Government
and credit institutions and a comparison of these policies
with credit policies in other sectors.
10.       The Consultant shall assess and recommend institutional changes
necessary to implement Government's programs for development of the private
agricultural sector, including:
a.   establishment of credit institutions, marketing agencies
and information services, and irrigation requirements; and
b.   determination of the organization, management, financial
structure, and types of services to be offered to private
farmers that are necessary to maximize their production.
I 11.      Using data and other information generated from the project, as
well as from other sources, the Consultant shall prepare a feasibility study
for a private farm credit project.  The study shall include an examination
of potential markets for production from the proposed project, and the pre-
paration of:
a.   representative models, including technical, operational
and financial aspects, for both the private and social
sectors in the Republic of Macedonia;
b.   annual operating costs, revenues, subsidies, investments,
loans and amortization thereof, and overall cash flow
projections;
Ce   cost-benefit analysis, including the financial rate of
return, on proposed net investments;
d.   detailed management and organizational structure for the
execution of the project;
e.   proposed project implementation time schedule; and
f.   lists of goods and services with unit prices for all major
items of the proposed project, identifying the local and
foreign currency components for each item and showing
import duties and subsidies as separate entries.



ANNEX 5
Page 13
12.       The Consultant shall undertake a financial and economic evaluation
of the proposed project, including total cost estimates and detailed work
programs. Specifically included shall be:
a.   financial forecasts and analyses of project costs for
inputs and supporting services. Operating revenues and
expense statements, investment forecasts and cash flow
statements shall also be prepared;
b.   estimates and analysis of the net economic benefits.  In
addition to the cost and benefit information available
from the models (para 11), the net economic benefits shall,
where applicable, include estimates and analysiL of taxes,
subsidies, alleviation of underemployment, and the costs
of technical services necessary for implementation of the
proposed project. Overall economic evaluation shall include
the determination of the economic rate of return on project
investment using the discounting to present values method.
Detailed schedules showing phasing and timing of all project
investments and expected subborrower participation shall
be prepared.
13.       The Consultant shall cooperate fully with SBS and Government,
which shall provide the necessary data, facilities and services outlined in
paras 16 and 17. The Consultant shall be solely responsible for the analyses
and interpretation of data received and for the findings, conclusions and
recommendations contained in the reports. SBS shall facilitate the liaison
with the consultant responsible for the marketing study to ensure that the
Consultant has access to all information necessary to complete the study.
14.       The Consultant shall complete the operations defined in paras 9
through 12 within 2 years of appointment.
15.       The Consultant shall submit the following reports to SBS:
a.   Monitoring and Evaluation of Project
(i) Progress reports - annual monitoring report for 5 years,
except as provided for in para 7 (20 copies in English);
(ii)  Draft final report - within 90 days from completion
(20 copies in English); and
(iii) Final report - within 30 days from receipt of SBS's
comments (50 copies in English).
b.   Feasibility Study for Development of Private Farm Sector
(i) Draft feasibility report - within 60 days after
completion (20 copies in English); and



ANNEX 5
Page 14
(ii) Final feasibility report - within 30 days after receipt
of SBS's comments (50 copies in English).
16.       In addition to the above, the Consultant shall furnish SBS with
such information as it may reasonably request to follow progress of the study.
All reports and documents will become the exclusive property of the Govern-
ment of Macedonia.
17.       SBS and the Government of Macedonia would furnish the consultants
all relevant statistical data, maps and other information available. The
consultants would not publish or disclose any of the information so obtained
without written consent of SBS.
18.       SBS and Government's assignment of clerical assistance, tech-
nicians, professionals, office accommodations, vehicles and local equipment
would be mutually agreed upon during contract negotiations.
19.       The proposed study is estimated to cost about US$150,000 and
would require about 72 man-months consisting of the following expetise:
a.   Agricultural Credit Expert - project leader, responsible for
organization of the studies, liaison with other marketing
and irrigation studies consultants, etc. This person should
have long experience in agricultural credit particularly to
small farmers, credit institutions, Government planning
operations and organizational problems.
b.   Agriculturist
C.   Agricultural Economist
d.   Financial Analyst
e.   Credit Specialist
f.   Rural Development Specialist
g.   Statistician.
h.   Marketing Specialist
i.   Livestock Specialist

ANNEX 9
Page 1
YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
Production and Marketing of Project Commodities
*  1.     As discussed in Chapter V, the main products produced under the
project would be milk, beef and pork meat, table grapes, wine, fish and
tobacco. Table 1 summarizes lacedonia's present production of these products,
and the production and consumption projections for 1975 and 1980.
Milk
2.        Present production of dairy products in Macedonia is estimated at
about 103,000 tons (Table 1), which is expected to increase without the proj-
ect to about 107,000 tons by 1975, the main increase coming from cow milk
products. As mentioned in Annex 1, Government is increasing its efforts to
improve farm production and it is expected that the total dairy cattle herd
will increase from about 282,000 head in 1971 to about 320,000 head in 1975.
Sheep milk production is expected to decrease since the number of sheep have
been declining because of the reduced demand for sheep milk.
3.        Present consumption of dairy products in Macedonia is about 122,000
tons which is equivalent to about 72 kg per person per annum (Macedonia's
population is about 1.7 million at present), slightly below Yugoslavia's
average-of 80 kg per person. It is expected that the total consumption
would increase by about 3.5% per annum, since population growth is about
1.4%; and the consumption increase because of increased per capita income
would be about 2.0% (income elasticity is about 0.5% and per capita income
increase is estimated at about 4% per annum). Macedonia's present deficit
of about 19,000 tons of milk products is imported from neighboring republics
and would grow to about 26,000 tons by 1975 and to about 3i,000 tons by 1980.
The expected project production of 24 million 1 from the social sector and
4 million 1 from private farms, for a total of about 28 million 1 of milk
(equivalent to about 23,000 tons of diary products based on the same pro-
portion of consumption of products as present usage), can therefore easily
be sold on the Macedonia market or to other republics. Yugoslavia is a
net importer of about 19,000 tons of dairy products.
Fresh and Processed Meat
4.        Macedonia's present production of fresh and processed meat is
about 23,000 tons, while present consumption is about 34,000 tons, result-
ing in a deficit of about 11,000 tons. Production of slaughter cattle and
pigs is expected to grow substantially because a number of combinats have
established new farm units. The other types of meat are expected to in-
crease at a normal rate of about 3.5% per annum.



ANNEX 9
Page  2
5.        The present consumption of about 34,000 tons is expected to grow
to about 41,000 tons by 1975, representing a growth rate of about 4.9% per
annum. This growth rate is below the national average (about 7.0%) and has
been derived as follows:
(a)  Population growth                          1.4%
(b) A demand growth of about 3.5% based
on a per capita income growth of 4%
and an income elasticity of demand of
about 0.85%                                3.5%
4.9% per annum
By 1975, with a projected production of 34,000 tons, the shortage of meat
products without the project would therefore be about 7,000 tons (Table 1).
Under the proposed project the total incremental production of fresh and
processed meat would be about 4,300 tons 1/, and there would, tharefore, be
no difficulty in marketing the additional production in Macedonia. Never-
theless, because there is a world meat shortage, it would be advantageous
for Macedonia to export part of its production of high quality meats and
to import inferior quality types of meat, and it is expected that about
2,000 tons of the project's output would be exported.
6.        Yugoslavia as a whole exports about 127,000 tons of fresh and pro-
cessed meat or about 16% of its total production of 774,000 tons (Table 2).
Its major markets for beef, veal and pork are Italy, Greece, and Austria and
are further discussed below.
7.        Export Markets-Beef and Veal.  Currently, Yugoslavia exports beef
and veal mainly to Italy (75%) and Greece. It is expected that demand from
these countries as well as from others will continue to be strong. At pre-
sent, North America, the EEC and other Western countries are about 1.5 million
tons in deficit for beef and veal, and according to FAO projections, this
deficit is expected to rise to 3,024,000 tons by 1980. All of the increases
in demand have occurred and would continue to occur in the 'ace of rising prices
of beef and veal on the international markets.  The price --and continues
upwards with early 1972 prices already ranging from US$1,200/ton for boned
forequarters beef imported into Europe to over US$2,000/ton for baby beef
carcasses imported into France and over US$2,500/ton for baby beef special
cuts cif Italy.  As special cuts are the major fresh beef export under
the project, a price of about US$2,300 (fob) was used for model.
8.        Export Markets-Pork.  Yugoslavia exports at present about 48,000
tons of pork (Table 2), 60% of which is processed pork, mainly to Italy and
Austria. While world demand for pork has only grown slowly in the past, it
1/   The total output of the meat processing plant would be about 11,000
tons of meat; however, about 6,700 tons would be existi-ig production,
processed at present in an inferior way.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
is expected to increase more rapidly in the future because of the shortage
of beef and veal meat. The Western European increase in consumption in
recent years has been about 3%, which is expected to increase to 6% for the
period 1975-1980. Yugoslavia would be in a good position to increase its
share in the export markets for pork.
9.        Processing of pork enables the marketer to apply the classical
elements of product differentiation to his products and thus to target to
specific market segments. Thus, oversimplifying pork demand does not take
account of the apparently rising demand for boneless, low-fat, canned re-
frigerated ham and ham products. These products, exported from Poland, the
Netherlands and Denmark, and now from Yugoslavia, have commanded high prices
on the U.K. and U.S.A. markets.
10.       Since the proposed project facilities would meet all the inter-
national hygienic standards, Macedonia would be in an excellent position to
increase its exports, particularly of processed meat and pork products. Most
of Macedonia's meat products are transported in refrigerated trucks and the
trucking industry is well organized and has sufficient capacity to meet
future increases. The Federal Government is going to monitor Yugoslavia's
exports of meat products in order to control quality and will sell mainly
to commission agents in importing countries. It does not intend, as it is
doing for fruits and vegetables, to establish its own marketing agencies
since the commission agent system works well for meat products.
Table Grapes
11.       Macedonia's present annual production of table grapes is about
56,000 tons, the main part of which (31,000 tons) is sold outside the Re-
public. Macedonia's present consumption is about 10 kg per person per annum
(17,000 tons), which is lower than the national average of about 11 kg per
person per annum. Production in Macedonia without the project is expected
to increase at the rate of about 3.5% per annum, while consumption in Macedonia
would grow by about 3.2% per annum until 1975 (1.4% population growth, 1.8%
income demand growth) and by about 3.3% per annum from 1975 to 1980 (popula-
tion growth 1.3% and income demand growth about 2.0%). The surplus in
Macedonia's production by 1980 would be about 53,000 tons (Table 1). With
the project production of about 20,000 tons, Macedonia would have to market
about 73,000 tons of table grapes.
12.       It is expected that almost all of Macedonia's surplus would be sold
in the other Republics. Table 3 shows the supply-demand balance for Macedonia
and Yugoslavia as a whole for table grapes and grape juices. By 1980, Yugo-
slavia's total demand including potential exports would be about 277,000 tons
of grapes while total production including project production would only be
about 270,000 tons. This estimate is conservative because it has been assumed
that the production of table grapes in the republics other tnan Macedonia
would remain at the present level. In fact, the last years have shown a de-
clining trend in the other republics which is expected to continue because
farmers there have a comparative advantage in producing wine grapes and are
hence switching from table grapes. This trend is also occurring in countries



ANNEX 9
Page  4
such as Italy and Portugal and has occurred in Austria and West Germany which
presently do not produce any table grapes. Macedonia, with its excellent
climate and soils for table grapes production, will therefore have no difficulty
in marketing its surplus production.
13.       The total incremental production under the project would be about
20,000 tons (17,600 tons from the social sector, 2,800 tons from private
farmers), of which probably about 2,000 tons would be exported. By 1980,
grape exports from Yugoslavia would probably account for no more than 5% of
the country's total production. The reason that Macedonia would have a higher
export/production percentage is that its table grapes are in high demand for
their quality and taste.
14.       Yugoslavia's main export markets are Austria (about 70%) and West
Germany. Although Italy is the main supplier of these markets and is in an
advantageous position vis-a-vis West Germany because of its membership in the
EEC, it is expected that Yugoslavia will be able to keep its competitive posi-
tion. Yugoslav table grapes, and especially Macedonian varieties, have com-
peted very well in the export markets on grounds of price and taste. Also,
since the number of tourists visiting Yugoslavia account for a substantial
part of the demand for Yugoslav table grapes in their home countries and
since the number of Austrian and West German tourists to Yugoslavia is ex-
pected to double in the next ten years, Yugoslavia is expected to continue
to have good export markets.  In addition, the Federal Government is making
a conscientious effort to regulate the export market.  At present, the dif-
ferent republics in Yugoslavia are competing with each other and, therefore,
the Federal Government has decided to establish fruit and vegetable agencies
in the different European markets to coordinate exports from the country as
a whole.
15.       Most shipments of fruits and vegetables to Austria and West Germany
are made by refrigerated truck and the trucking industry is efficient. Class-
ification and grading of fruits for export is undertaken by the Federal Gov-
ernment inspection service at points of embarkation and grade standards are
in general similar to those in the U.S. If the fruits fail to meet the export
standards when inspected at point of embarkation, they are rejected.
16.       Seasonality is the strongest single factor in the price determina-
tion  . table grapes in export markets.  Prices in Western Europe are high
at the commencement of the off-season production period from January to early
July, fall sharply in late July and hit bottom in September/October when
supplies are at their peak. Macedonia, as has been mentioned, has no season-
ality advantage, and it can expect to receive only the average full-season
price, which in 1971 was about US$0.27 per kg fob border importing coun-
tries. Although this price is expected to rise slightly in the future, for
analysis purposes the 1971 export price and corresponding ex-farm price have
been used.
.



ANNEX 9
Page 5
Wine
17.       Macedonia's production of about 59 million liters of wine accounts
for about 11% of Yugoslavia's total wine production of about 555 million
liters. It is not known how much of Macedonia's production is consumed in
the Republic, in other republics or exported, but it is known that Yugoslavia's
wine export is to a large extent based on wines of Macedonian origin.
18.       Yugoslavia as a whole exported in 1971 about 57 million liters of
wines, mainly to the U.K., Austria and West Germany. In the last four years
Yugoslavia's exports have increased by more than 20% per anxnum. One of the
main reasons for this increase is that tourists continue to consume Yugoslav
wines after they have returned to their home countries. As has been mentioned,
it is expected that the numbers of tourists to Yugoslavia would double within
the next ten years. There is no doubt that Yugoslavia, provided it could
produce it, could export substantially larger quantities in the future. How-
ever, domestic demand is also increasing at a fast pace and projections made
by FAO show that Yugoslavia's exports will probably decrease because of the
increased home demand (see table below).
Comparison of Wine Production and Consumption - Yugoslavia

/1   Actual
19.       Macedonia's incremental production under the project would be about
19 million liters of wine, although the total production cef the wine processing
plant would be about 40 million liters. The reason for this difference is that
about 13 million liters of wine would be bought from other combinats' present
production, and about 8 million liters from the private sector's present pro-
duction. The cellar's incremental production would come mainly from the
crushing of grapes (about 28,400 tons) from combinats' new vineyards.
20.       The present percentage of Yugoslavia's exports to its production
is about 10%. Because of the strong home demand, it has been assumed that
Macedonia's exports under the project would not rise above this percentage
even though Macedonian wines are famous for their quality. Of the total
incremental production probably about 2 - 2.5 million liters would thus be
exported and about 16.5 - 17.0 million liters would be sold domestically.



ANNEX 9
Page  6
21.       Yugoslavia's wine prices, especially of bulk wine, have increased
slightly in recent years. For instance, between 1969 and 1972 the U.K. price
of Yugoslav bulk wine increased from ND 4.3 per liter to about ND 4.9 per
liter (cif), and this trend is expected to continue. However, for the
analysis in this report, 1972 prices (ND 3.77 liter fob Yugoslav border)
have been used in order to calculate the financial returns conservatively.
Wine by-products
22.       The total incremental production from the two plants would consist
of: 10,000 tons of vinegar; 7,500 tons of livestock feed, 1,500 tons grape
seed, 730 tons alcohol wine distillate, 560 tons tartaric acid and wine acid
and 100 tons& baking powder. There would be no problem in marketing these
quantities.  The livestock feed would be sold to the dairy cattle fattening,
heifer rearing and pig units. Regarding distillates, acids and baking powder,
Yugoslavia is importing substantially larger quantities than would be pro-
duced under the project. Regarding vinegar, no exact data are available
because the Federal Bureau of Statistics includes it in the group of non-
alcoholic drinks for which it has no breakdown. However, all market experts
are of the opinion that Yugoslavia is at present poorly supplied with vinegar.
For instance, many consumers use artificially produced wine acid instead of
vinegar.  Moreover, vinegar is also in increasing demand by the growing can-
ning industry.
Fish
23.       Macedonia's present production of 900 tons of fish accounts for only
a very smallI portion of the country's total production of 19,000 tons (of which
12,000 tons is carp). As indicated in Table 1, Macedonia has at present a
deficit of about 3,000 tons, which is expected to grow to about 3,400 tons by
1975 and 5,100 tons by 1980. Also the country as a whole has a deficit. Yugo-
slavia imported about 23,000 tons of fish and fish products in 1970 and exported
about 12,000 tons. The incremental production under the project of about 1,500
tons can, therefore, easily be sold.  The average sales pricct: ex-pond is about
ND 10/kg for carp and ND 35/kg for trout.
Tobacco
24.       Macedonia's annual production of about 24,000 tons (20,000 tons
Oriental type, 4,000 tons Virginia type tobacco) accounts for 48% of Yugo-
slavia's total production of about 49,000 tons. Macedonia and the rest of
Yugosiavia produce mainly Oriental and semi-Oriental tobaccos (about 40,000
tons) and rank fifth in world production of this variety. The remainder of
Yugoslavia's production, about 9,000 tons, consists of Virginia tobaccos.
Yugoslavia as a whole exported in 1971 about 19,000 tons of Oriental and
semi-Oriental tobacco and imported about 5,500 tons of Virginia tobacco
(about double that of the 1969 figure).
.



ANNEX 9
Page 7
Is        25.        It is expected that Yugoslavia will need to import more Virginia
type tobaccos since cigarette consumption is increasing at a high rate (by
about 4% per annum) and Virginia tobacco represents an essential part of the
cigarette blend. Moreover, the Virginia type of cigarette compared to the
Oriental type is increasing in popularity. As a result, Yugoslavia will
need some additional 1,000 - 1,500 tons of Virginia tobacco each year, of
which Macedonia would need about 20% since it accounts for this portion of
Yugoslavia's total tobacco products production.
26.       Macedonia, compared to the other republics, is in a very advantageous
position for growing Virginia tobacco because of its climate and soils, provid-
ed it has sufficient irrigation and drying facilities.  Under the proposed pro-
ject, the incremental production of Virginia tobacco on existing irrigated lands
at full development would be about 1,400 tons per annum for which dryers would
be provided. The increased annual production of Virginia tobacco would sub-
stitute for imports (valued at US$ 1.26 million equivalent, at the 1972 f.o.b.
border price). The prices of both Virginia and Oriental type tobaccos have
fluctuated in recent years, but have averaged about US$1.25 and US$ 0.90 per
kg f.o.b. border for Oriental and Virginia tobacco, respectively.
27.       Producer prices of tobacco were formerly supported by subsidies
from the Federal Government but these were abolished in September 1971. Since
then, tobacco prices have been placed under the guaranteed minimum price sup-
port scheme, whereby prices are determined by producers, cooperatives and
tobacco enterprises in line with market prices, subject to the minimum prices
set forth in the inter-Republic agreements.  In fact, the guaranteed minimum
price support has meant little in recent years, since prices bf tobacco have
been above the Government minimum price levels.
0

YUGOSLAVIA
AGRICULTURAL INDUSTRIES PROJECT - MACEDONIA
Operating Results
1.        The project would help finance subprojects in both the social and
the private sectors. In the social sector integrated agricultural industries
enterprises (combinats) would receive loans for: (i) the expansion of their
livestock industry by about 12,800 head of cattle--5,000 dairy, 5,300 fatten-
ing and 2,500 rearing--and by about 40,000 pigs; (ii) the construction of a
modern meat processing plant (with an annual capacity of 12,000 head of cattle
and 125,000 pigs); (iii) the establishment of vineyards (2,AOO ha); (iv) the
construction of wine processing facilities (with an annual pzoduction equiva-
lent to 40 million liters of wine and about 20,000 tons of wine by-products);
(v) the construction of cold storage and grain silo facilities (with a total
of 17,500 tons of static capacity); and (vi) the development of fishponds
(with an annual production of 1,500 tons of carp and 75 tons of trout). In
the private sector, private farmers would receive credit for (vii) on-farm
improvements, mainly for the construction of tobacco drying sheds (150 flue-
curing units), for table grape farm improvements, and for the upgrading of
dairy cattle.
2.        The estimated financial rates of return presented in Appendices 1
through 17 are as follows:

3.        The principal assumptions used in the models are the following:
Dairying (Appendix 1)
4.        There would be five units, each having a herd of 1,000 cows.  The
major revenue would consist of milk sales, estimated at full development in
year 4 at 4.8 million 1 per unit, sold at a price of ND 2.6 per 1. Feed
(mainly maize, fodder and alfalfa) would form the major component of the
operating expenses and would be produced by each combinat. Further details
are in Appendix 1.
Cattle Fattening (Appendix 2)
5.        Each unit of 1,000 head (there would be four units) wouLd sell 1,320
head of fattened cattle per year since it would take about nine months to fatten
the cattle. The cattle would be bought at 110 kg per head for a price of ND
1,760 per head and sold at 435 kg per head for a price of ND 6,000 per head.
Major oper4ting expenses, in addition to calf purchases, are again the cost of
feed.
Heifer Rearing (Appendix 3)
6.        There would be four units.  Each unit of 1,000 head would sell per
annum 540 bred heifers and 60 non-breeders since the heifers would be bought
at 3 months of age, bred at 15-16 months and sold at about 23 months. The
sale price would be about ND 11,000 for an in-calf heifer. Feed is again the
mnjor componen-_ of operating expenses followed by heifer calf purchases.
Pig Breeding/Fattening (Appendix 4)
7.        The basic herd, which consists of 526 sows farrowing 2.3 times per
year, would provide about 10,000 fattened pigs per year for sale at a price
of ND 1,000 per head. Major operating expenses are feed. There would be two
units undeg this project, one of 10,000 head as mentioned above and the other
of 30,000 head. Because of economies of scale, the rate of return of the
larger unit would be better than that of the smaller unit.



ANNEX 10
Page 3
Meat Processing (Appendix 5)
8.        The meat processing plant would produce, at full development in
year 5, about 600 tons of fresh special meat cuts for export (sale price
ND 40/kg), 1,300 tons of fresh meat for the local market (ND 22.50/kg),
1,800 tons of canned meat for export (ND 23/kg), and 7,300 tons of canned
meat for the local market (ND 20/kg), for a total of about 11,000 tons of
meat and 3,200 tons of by-products (hair, hides, edible lard, bone and meat
meal, etc.). Total number of cattle and pigs processed at full production
would be about 12,000 and 125,000, respectively. Details of the operating
costs are in Appendix 5B.
Vineyeards (Appendices 6 and 7)
9.        Ten combinats would participate in the vineyard program for a total
of 1,600 ha of wine grapes and 1,000 ha of table grapes. Total production
would be 24,000 tons and 22,000 tons, respectively. The major part (80%) of
the table grapes would be sold in Yugoslavia for local consumption; the wine
grapes and the remainder of the table grapes would be sold to wineries. Sales
prices have been conservatively estimated.
Wine Processing (Appendix 8)
10.       There would be only one plant under this project and its major pur-
pose would be to improve the quality of Macedonia's inferior type wines by
blending, aging and stablizing. The plant would buy wines from existing small
wineries. Its annual input would be equivalent to 60,000 tons of grapes and
its output would be equivalent to about 40 million 1 of wine (38 million I of
wine and brandy and 4 million 1 of carbonated drinks). The major operating
costs are the raw materials (grape purchase price ND 1.80 per kg). Sales
prices are indicated in Appendix 8.
Wine By-products (Appendix 9)
11.       There would be two units, each with different end products.  Inputs
in both cases would be 10,000 tons of marc (the pressed cake left over from
grape pressing) and 2,000 tons of lees (the residue after fermentation of
wines). Principal products--a total of about 20,000 tons ^;,: the two units--
would be vinegar, livestock feed, tartaric acid and its derivatives, baking
powder, and distillates. The Tikves unit has a higher rate of return than
the UACM unit because the investments would be incremental to its existing
operations.
Cold Store (Appendix 10)
12.       Two units would be constructed, each of 5,000 tons static capacity,
for extending the marketability of fruits and vegetables and dairy and meat
products. The revenues are based on a fee basis of ND 200 per ton per month
and a 65% utilization rate.



ANNEX 10
Page 4
Grain Storage Silos (Appendices 11 and 12)
13.       Two units, one for corn (capacity 5,000 tons) and the other for
rice (capacity 2,500 tons), would provide storage for increased production
resulting from the on-going government irrigation schemes.  In the case of
corn storage, the combinat would save about ND 0.23 per kg on a total pro-
duction of 5,000 tons. In the case of rice storage, revenues are based on
a fee of ND 500 per ton per annum and a capacity utilization rate of 50%.
Fishponds (Appendices 13 and 14)
14.       There would be five units of 200 ha each for carp (eachi unit pro-
ducing 300 tons) and two units for trout (producing 15 tons and 60 tons,
respectively). Total production of all units would be 1,500 tons of carp
and 75 tons of trout.
Private Farmers--Tobacco Dryer (Appendix 15)
15.       The total number of tobacco drying units would be 150, each drying
about 10 tons of V.'irginia tobacco per year for a total of 1,500 tons. The
benefits would es,nsist of two parts:
(i)  the normal drying fee accruing to the operators                           0
(farmers' cooperatives) of ND 8.7 per kg of tobacco;
(ii)  the incremental production of farmers.  With the dryers
farmers can produce Virginia tobacco instead of Oriental
tobacco and thus produce about 650 kg per farm per year
additionally.
Private Farmers--Table Grapes (Appendix 16)
ib.       The rotal investments per ha would be about ND 43,C00  (US.'2,500).
At full development in year 6, annual production would be about 20 tons/ha
on irrigated land and about 13 tons/ha on non-irrigated land. Table grapes
would be sold at a price of ND 2.2 per kg (80%) and ND 0.8 per kg (20%).
Private Farmers--Dairying(Appendix 17)
i7.       liAn illustrative model has been drawn up for a 5-cow unit which
would produce about 12,000 1 per annum at a sales price of ND 2.6 per 1 for
a total gross revenue of ND 31,000 per annum. Major operating costs would
include feed costs. The net income would be about ND 14,000 per annum,
while total investment costs would be about ND 73,000.
.
